O children, since I have fulfilled this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, by its completion in it, and by its expositions and declarations, it has appeared to me that you will complete that with this treatise which is a part of surgery through manual operation.
For the operation through manual technique is proscribed in our realm and in our times deprived of all, while greatly endangering the science of it, and its procedures are hindered; and nothing remains of it but some slight descriptions in the books of the Ancients which they have altered and led to those errors and misconceptions whereby the understanding of it is clouded and its power and art prolonged.
And it has seemed to me that you enliven that through the arrangement of this treatise according to its second way of exposition and declaration and abridgement; and so that the forms of the instruments of cautery and of the other tools of the work come through addition of declaration, and through preparation of it, which cause is necessary.
But the cause whereby no good artificer is found by the hand in our times is because the art of medicine is long, and that one who practices it before he be exercised in the science of anatomy, which belongs to Gualia, knowing the parts of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and having the knowledge of the bones, the nerves and the sinews, and the number of these, and their issue; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and the places of their issue.
And on account of this Hypocras says that one whose name is great but in action small, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said of this in the introduction to this book; for who is not learned in that which we have said concerning anatomy, it is certain that he will fall into error whereby men will die, as I have seen many of these, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves from that without learning or experience.
For I saw a foolish and experienced physician incise a scrofulous abscess on the neck of a woman, and incised some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed and the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who, when he had extracted a stone from a man whose condition was improved, and the stone was large, came to him and drew out that stone with a piece of the bladder so that the man died on the third day.
And I called to them to draw it out and saw that on account of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received stipends from a duke of our land for surgery, and he undertook to treat a negro who had suffered a fracture in his thigh; and preparing with his ignorance, drew out the fracture over the wound with powders and splints with strong compression and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And he let him loose according to his desires and bound him and left him for several days and commanded that we unbind the ligature; and it was thus until the thigh and the leg were apostematized, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him and made preparation to unbind the ligature, and tranquility was achieved and he was eased of his pains.
But the corruption was nonetheless confirmed in the member so that I could not restrain it and the corruption continued to circulate in the member until the patient died.
And I saw another physician who perforated a cankerous abscess and it was ulcerated for several days until the evil of it grew great with its lord.
The cause of which is that the cold humor is pure, and it does not come to him by iron at all but that it be in a member which suffers that it be opposed to all things.
And on account of this, children, it is necessary for you that the operation through manual technique be considered in two ways: one is in operation which is associated with health, and the other in work whereby there is peril in the greatest dispositions.
And I have already expounded that in every place of this book in which occurs operation whereby there is error and fear; so that it is necessary for you to guard against that and leave it, lest the foolish bring about your harm through speaking.
And therefore take up your weapons with diligence and with God's protection, and your patients with gentleness and firmness.
And use the best way through God's grace for the sake of health and praiseworthy success and rid yourselves of terrible illnesses of difficult cure, and arm yourselves against that which causes you to fear so that trembling in your faith and in your name does not abandon you: for more remains for your glory and beyond in the world and in the last days for your saints.
Galia already said in some of his books: "Do not heal a bad illness lest bad physicians be named."
And I have arranged this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered concerning the feet, in the forms of the instruments and the tools of the cauteries, and of that which is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of bleeding and piercing, and of cupping-glasses, and of wounds, and of extraction of arrows and their likes, all capitulated and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of reduction and of dislocation, and of cure for twisting and their likes; a chapter ordered concerning the feet and the forms of the instruments.
That I say the quality of the humors of that cautery and of the nocent things in it, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the speech of the quality of the humor of cautery and of the nocent thing in it is long and prolixed, and subtle through science, and secret hid away; and already many wise men have spoken greatly of it, and from it they have disagreed, and for me a little of their speeches suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery confers universally on all complexions, save two complexions which are complexions of coldness without matter.
In the complexions of coldness and dryness with matter they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial for it, and the others said the contrary of this, that cautery is not convenient in illnesses which are by quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that an illness of coldness and dryness be cauterized.
And he said the contrary of this that through cautery benefit is done in every illness of coldness and dryness which one sees in the bodies of men for when you compare the body of man and its moistures to the complexions of fire, you moisten the body of man.
And I say by the speech of that which, that experiment that already many times I have discovered.
But it is not necessary to come over here that which is already demonstrated and exercised in the chapter of cauteries by previous exercise, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men, and the disposition of diseases in their bodies, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear over you, and especially in the moist and friction diseases, for all the doctors have agreed upon those, and are not disagreeing upon using cautery on them.
And know, children, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and its goodness, is that it is a simple substance, whose action does not reach any other member except the one being cauterized, and does not affect another member with it except with minor harm.
But the action of the burning medicine reaches those members from which it is distant, and strongly brings about disease in the member difficult to heal, and strongly causes it.
But the fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do that except to make superfluous. And it is known to us through experiment and through the longevity of service of study on the truth of causes; and because of this I am excused from the length of speech. And if it were not that I was not read in this book to you, secretly hidden by the fire, and the quality of its operation on the bodies and its expulsion by which you expel diseases, with philosophical speech demonstrated, which would be acute in your ears.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time in which it is appropriate to perform cautery and pause at the best time of spring.
And I say that cautery is convenient in all times, because the harm by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of that which is attracted by the cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pain or necessary pains, strong and violent, which do not tolerate delay so that they fear the evil that comes, which is greater than minor harm coming from the time.
And you should not listen to them, O children, or they will not believe it except the common people and foolish doctors; so it is that for this disease which is healed by cautery there is no reduction in all times, and they pause at that consequence.
And the cause is not as they think it to be, for cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries up the moistness which are the cause by which pain comes.
But cautery surpasses medicine in value and power of its operation, and grandeur of his lordship.
And it is possible that his disease will return at some time of the year, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his disease, and its power, and what has been prepared in his body by the accumulation of excesses in him, and what has come upon him through acquisition in foods and like those causes; but if the disease in which cautery was administered is a subtle disease in a member with few excesses and moistness, so that cautery of teeth for pain and its likes, it is possible that the pain will not return in him, but that happens little.
And it is a common saying that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a true saying, not that they understand it to be, for they believe that there is no healing that benefits through medicine or other cause except cautery.
And the cause is in contrast to that; and the intention that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is not unless the manners of healing are administered in some diseases, and those medicines do not confer, and then at the end of the cause the man is administered cautery, and benefits from it. And from here we see that cautery is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the vulgar and foolish doctors understand it.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say it except for the equality of gold and sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make it strong, and that is not absolutely true, for I have already proved and demonstrated that it does not do that except in some bodies without the others, and cautery with it is good and better than with iron as they said; but if you perform cautery with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it is made according to the amount you wish according to the roughness of the gold, and it prepares itself to receive friction; and if you apply ignition on it, the gold melts by the fire, and in the fire some sheets are placed so that the artificer sees through the occupation of that.
And because of that cautery with iron is made more lightly and closer to the correctness of operation.
And I have already arranged this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and have arranged those of the head among the feet, so that elsewhere I will ask him what he wants from it.
The first chapter:
This cauterization confers to moisture and coldness the accidental servant of which there are two causes, and it is caused by soda and multitude of flux, called neuritis, from the head to the parts of the ears and from the eyelashes flowing, and multitude of sleep, and of toothache and of gum inflammation, and universally of all disease that comes through friction, such as paralysis, lethargy, and apoplexy, and like in those diseases.
The form of this cauterization is this: first of all command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine the head cleansing, for three or four days, according to that the power of the patient and his age and habit.
And then command that he shaves his head with a razor, and then let him sit between your hands on a heated bench, and let him rest his hands on his feet.
And then pause the edge of your palm, and then put your fingers between your eyes. But however, when that part of the middle location has been reached, mark it with a tent, and then ignite the olive-oil cautery, of which this is the form:
And then put that on the marked location, in such a way that it descends, and your hand be slightly pressed there, and you revolve it.
And then lift your hand slightly and look at the location, and if you see already some quantity of the head uncovered, as well as the amount of hair, then lift your hand again, and if it has not returned your hand with that iron, with another, if it is cooled, while it is seen by you what I have told you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the location, and let that be for three days.
And then put on it cotton soaked in vinegar and let that be on it until the scar of the fire is destroyed. And then heal the location with tetropharmacon unguent while it is healed.
And they said that when the Roman plaster is wet, then as many herbs are put on it, for that is better and more beneficial.
And some of them said that the jaw should be cauterized within the bone, and have the cautery within it until some part of the substance of the bone is burnt.
And then let that be scratched that which is burnt in the bone, and then let it be healed.
Others said that they reached the bone with the cautery before they put pressure on the strong bones, and this is the form of little Alcphatis. And they think that from that place they expel the vapors from the head.
And if the ulcer has been open for a long time and is not healing, it should be treated until it is healed.
But I have not seen these two kinds of cautery at all, except in some men according to the way of fear; and that one, as far as I am concerned, is a thousand times better for health when it is; because the head is devitalized by solution of continuity with him as we have witnessed in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a frail nature.
And the first kind of cautery is more safe and better as far as I am concerned; and that one should be used for health, both its use and its work.
Chapter second.
Of cautery to the head.
When in all the head there is a chronic and long-standing pain which is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping glasses and caput medusae and plasters and poultices and bandages and cauteries and similar remedies, and especially if he has already been cauterized with one cautery which does not benefit him at all from those causes, then consider that if the patient's head is of a strong creation in its nature; and it is not frail but finds great pain from great heat, then cauterize it with another cautery after that first one. Then cauterize that above the crown of his head with a cautery until the scalp is split open. The scalp having been split and the quantity which we have mentioned having been opened up, cauterize that with a cautery in the back part of the head at the place which is called the foramen magnum —- and it is that place on which the ventouse is applied. And loosen your hand in it, and do not open the bone, for the patient will find much greater pain from that than from all the other cauteries of the head.
And I will tell you about this cautery in its place, because the cautery by which they cauterize the crown of the head and the occiput is much more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it.
Chapter third.
Of cautery for a new wound, not old.
When a sudden pain occurs in the front part of the head, or pain is extended to the eye, let it be evacuated with medicated washings, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have written about in the different diseases; and if all do not help, then cautery to it is in two ways: either cauterize with a sharp medicine that burns, or with iron.
But cautery with a burning medicine is that you take some alum and scrape it, and put the extremes of it on both sides of the head. Then cut into the site of the pain in the temple with a pointed lance until you have made a hole in it underneath the scalp, and cover it up.
And then tie it up over there with a bandage so that it might be tightly bound; and let it stay in that place for the space of three hours; and then loosen the bandage, and treat the alum, and let the ulcer remain for two or three days; and then put your hand over it, submerged in warm water, until the site becomes swollen.
And then cure that with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you want, do that with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cautery with iron according to this way: heat up the cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail-shaped cautery, because the head of it has the shape of a nail with some curve to it, and in the middle part of it there is a small protuberance.
And then place it over the site of the pain, and hold your hand so that it might not be burnt by the cautery which is underneath; and move the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that is burnt by the heat of the cautery according to the thickness of the scalp were as high as the size of your fingernail, and raise up your hand so that the artery underneath might not be burnt, so that there might not come a flow of blood.
And then moisten the cautery in salt water and place it over the site for three days; and then apply a poultice soaked with warm water, and cure that with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you want, cauterize with the edge of a scalpel according to the cautery, and beware of cutting into an artery in this new wound that is not old.
Chapter fourth.
Of cautery for an old wound.
When you cure a wound with what we have said about the previous treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not help, and you see for the disease that it is not sufficient to it what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then when you ignite the cautery cut until it turns white, place your hand over the site of the pain with a bandage and tighten that while you pierce the artery underneath and it comes out into the bone.
However it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the mandible, which is worn down by chewing, and that the ligament not be burnt and the nerve underneath it, and that no spasm come over it.
And if it is about the custody and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that it is cut, for there is danger in the cutting of it, especially for him who ignores what he does and is inexperienced and unskilled; and let the operation stop at the beginning.
And I will come with remembrance of the rule for the flow of blood accidentally from the artery according to its own way, in its own place in this free book.
And if you see that this disease does not help this cautery and you see the body of the patient tolerable, cauterize it in the middle part of the head as we have mentioned, and cure the ulcer until it is healed.
And if you want, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter about stretching of the artery with cautery having two edges, and this cautery is better and more suitable.
Chapter fifth.
Of cautery for pains in the ears.
When in the ear there is pain from heat and it is cured with caustic remedies and with the other treatment which I have written about in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then ignite the cautery, and this is that one which has the name puncta, of which this is the form:
And then prick it with the cautery and heat it, and let it be moved around the whole of the ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circle of whichever part if the pain is in whichever part. Lengthen it with the cautery from the rim of the ear little by little so that you do not touch the site with the tip.
And if the cautery of ten points in all of the ear, and it, and then cure the sites until they are healed.
Chapter sixth.
Of cautery for torture.
Torture which is cured and cautery does not help except with remedies of the spices which come from phlegm, according as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the area that hurts due to dryness or due to a spasm in the nerve.
When you treat this kind of torture with poultices and bandages and healing remedies, and don't see any improvement, then you must cauterize the patient in three places: first at the root of the auricle, second a little below his temple, and third at the junction of the two lips.
And apply your cautery opposite to where it hurts because softening only comes from the side that is healing.
And the way to perform cauterization is to cauterize it on the right side of the highest part of the auricle a little bit above the corner of the head, and another one at the temple, whose length should be according to the length of the finger, and hold your hand with the cautery until half of the thickness of the hair is burnt.
And this is the form of the cautery, it is a kind of knife whose shape is described above. However, one should be more careful because it cuts deep in the skin. And if there are too many bumps on the surface, cut them a little bit.
Then treat the area with what is described above until it heals.
Chapter VII.
On cauterization for ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient and you treat it with what we have described above but don't see any improvement and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on all of the corners of his head; and with cautery on the middle part of the head as we have said before; and with cautery on the neck. And the way to perform cauterization is as it is described above.
And he has already been cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is more effective this way; and he will be cured with what we have described.
Chapter VIII.
On cauterization for oblivion caused by phlegm.
Give the patient large poultices and pills that are purifying at the beginning.
Then rub the whole head of the patient and place on his neck a plaster made from honey as it is described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply it a few times, because that is the way to perform cauterization. And do it according to what we have said above.
And if he heals well with this treatment, good; and if not, then do to him three cauteries in the back part of his head which should go from the highest part of the head down to the neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and treat the cauteries with what is described above.
However, if you see improvement and the patient tolerates it, then do to him cauterization in the middle part of his head; and if you see even more improvement, then cauterize him on both corners.
Then treat him until he is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped as it is described above.
Chapter IX.
On cauterization for a hernia and on softening of the whole body.
Give the patient poultices and treatment for the head as we have said above; then rub his head, and cauterize him with cautery in the middle part of the head, and with cautery on all of the corners of his head, and with cautery on the back of his neck, and with three cauteries on the spine of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body to give him trops and the patients tolerate it and the apoplexy is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the spine of the back, and lastly with cautery over his head until half of the thickness of the hair is burnt, and lift your hand. Then treat him until he is healed; and let this cautery be olive-shaped.
Chapter X.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless the cause of his epilepsy is phlegm.
And therefore give the patient treatments for the brain at the beginning of his illness with herbs and with other remedies as we have said in the division when the patient is strong and tolerates the excessive use of remedies.
And if the patient is a child who does not tolerate remedies, then administer healing poultices and masticables to him for several days with the benefit of foods.
Then rub the head of the patient, and cauterize him with a cautery in the middle part of the head as we have said before, and with another cautery on his occipital bone, and with cautery on all of the corners of his head. And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize him with cauteries as we have said before, having paralysis and softening of the body on the spine of the neck and the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped as it is described above.
However, if the patient is a child, then apply a subtle cautery according to this way:
Chapter XI.
On cauterization for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are moist and rotten humors and phlegm, then cauterize him with cauteries as we have said in having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is excess and decline to melancholy, and the body of the patient is moist, then give him to drink what purifies his brain as we have said in the division.
Then rub the head of the patient, and make a decently made turban out of linen and place it on his head, and then hold it in place.
And let the patient be seated, and let him be held upright.
Then apply a liniment; and it is more worthy of being made from old sheep's wool. And warm it over the fire with moderate heat as we have said when he is in him. Then apply it on the middle part of his head on the circle, and hold it in place until it is cool.
And do this to him once a week for several times, with the other treatment of him being good until he is healed; or if you want, cauterize him making small points according to his condition: this is a kind of cautery which softens the brain equally.
Then apply it wrapped in wool or in cotton wool.
Chapter XII.
On cauterization for water descending into the nose.
When you have declared the beginning of water in the nose due to the sinuses as we have said in the division, then start and give the patient to drink what purifies his head, and guard him from all moistness.
And let him sweat in a bath with vinegar for several days.
Then command him to wash his head, and cauterize him with cautery in the middle part of the head; and then cauterize him with two cauteries on two temples if it is the beginning of water in two noses, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one nose.
Strike with cautery all the veins and sinuses which are under the skin; and let the cauteries be long in length according to the length of the temples.
And guard against flowing of blood when he is cauterized; and if you see flowing of blood from him, stop it with some medicine which is possible for you.
I have come with wisdom that is in the extraction of arrows and in the bandaging of those [wounds] and the cauterization of blood flows.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the heads of the bones with two cauteries recently produced.
Chapter 13.
On the cauterization of ancient tears.
When tears of the eyes are old and frequent and the veins and arteries through which they flow are manifestly apparent on the surface of the head and it is verified that this is superfluous flowing phlegmatic fluids, then cauterize that with a cautery as we have recounted in the chapter on beginning of water gushing out in the eye, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries on the skull below the temples, and with two cauteries on the skull below the bones. And if it is necessary to add [to the cautery], then cauterize that in all sides of the eye socket over the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
On the cauterization of the stench of the nose.
When you are treating that with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not benefit, then you prepare and give to drink to the patient the cow's hoofs for three nights; and then shave his head and cauterize the middle of his head with a cautery made from an olive tree. And then cauterize that with a cautery made from a clove with two cauteries over the two eyebrows beneath the hair, a little one.
And be careful of the arrow that you do not burn it.
And of the cautery made from a clove, this is its form:
Chapter 15.
On the cauterization for softening of the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened due to sickness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery made from this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you like, cauterize that over the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in its entirety, and let them be elongated from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the amount of eyebrow.
And do not press too much with your hand with the cautery, but according to the amount that the upper part of the eyelid is attached.
Chapter 16.
On the cauterization of the eyelid when the hairs are converted to the parts within and close the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are converted to the parts within so that they close the eye, there are two ways of cauterizing them, either with a hot iron or with a burning medicine.
If with a cautery of fire, command the patient to look down at that so that he pulls his hairs down as they grow out and are cut off; and if it closes by itself, then you strengthen his eyes with a bandage so that he does not rub them as the hairs grow out.
Then when they are growing out and curling, then place the patient's head on your knee, and then draw a sign over the eyelid of his eyes with a pen, make the sign according to the shape of an olive leaf, and let the beginning of the sign be near the hairs.
And then place cotton dipped in white vinegar or in mullein tea on the eye.
And then light the cautery of which this is its form:
And then cauterize over the figure which you have drawn, bit by bit, in small spots, until the surface of the skin is burned black and appears like an olive leaf, all apparently even.
And a sign of the health of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs of it lifted up from the punctured eye.
And then raise your hand and lay him down for three days, and then place on him cotton with balms until the scar is healed. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if they return for some reason and the eyelid is softened, then do the cauterization on that again entirely as you had done at first.
But if the hairs are in the eyelid beneath it, then cauterize that until it is elevated to its natural place and curled back and the hairs do not poke the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you command the patient to look down at his hairs as they grow out and curl back, and then let it be made from linen the shape of an olive leaf.
And then take a pounded soap and of live coals a handful. [Chapter title:] Take that and mix it with the ashes, and then add to those ashes honey, and make that into a poultice so that it is not cold; and then spread that on the linen which is made in the shape of an olive leaf, and place that on one eye or both; and let the eye be closed with a bandage and the patient's head be in your lap.
And place the thumb on the medicine and press that a little and let him not rub it as he feels the burning of the medicine, for the burning is in him as if by fire.
And then when he perceives that the burning, let him look at the medicine and bite it with his teeth; then when the burning ceases, let him wash the eye with water and then consider if he sees the eyelid raised as it is washed by fire or by incision, for then it is well; and if it does not return to that of the medicine on the spot where the medicine has not been applied and is not blackened as your operation is completed and the eye is contracted.
And then place on him a bandage so that the scar of the wound is eradicated, and then cure that with an ointment of theriac or another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, after your operation, to watch diligently so that no pus falls into the eye from the medicine, and if the eyelid several days later is softened and some cause of the hairs poking into the eye, then repeat your medicine on that spot of the eyelid which is softened, and properly as you have done at the beginning, and then bandage him until he is healed.
And this is the shape of an olive leaf.
And know that men's eyes are diversified according to size and smallness, and therefore according to that is made your contraction.
And do not take the path of straightness to that to which it is directed in this art.
Chapter 17.
On the cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you are curing a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not agree, then know that you cauterize that according to this manner: command the patient to place his head on your lap, and let him hold his head by some servant so that it is not rubbed nor moved by his head.
And then place on the eye of him cotton dipped in white vinegar or in mullein tea.
And then light the cautery of which this is its form:
Which is hollow, like a reed or a feather made from a vulture, with one extremity from which the cautery is made.
If you wish to go further into the other extremity, then you can do so by using a tent.
However, the one who is concave is better for your work.
Then squeeze the fistula if it is open, and draw out from him pus and dry up that place. And if it is not open, then cut it open and extract the rottenness from him.
Then place on him the cautery which has been well lit, and have your hand close to the cautery at the start of the nose so that your hand does not get burned or the patient disturbed, and then apply the cautery on the gums of the hole and corrupt that place.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery it penetrates into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, as needed; and leave that there for three days, and then place on that spot a poultice and wrap it up with a bandage until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it has not healed, then apply to it a sharp, corrosive medicine so that the bone is uncovered and cut according to what suits you in your chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula which some people say at the beginning: enter into the place of the fistula and open it up, and then in that fissure place soft solder with which is its shape; and pour into that the quantity of one ounce of melted lead; and keep your hand with good retention so that the patient is not burned as the melted lead does not reach the hole of that; and cover what you have placed over the hole of the patient with cotton or water, for the lead adheres to the place of the fistula and heals it by a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, that is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a blow to the nose and reduce the fistula into the nose according to what will come into its proper place.
Chapter 28

Chapter 29
On cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula or in the palate or in the roots of the teeth, a pustule is formed and then bursts, and then becomes old, and pus and blood flow out, and there is a fistula, and you treat it and your treatment does not benefit, then when you ignite cautery according to the quantity that is received in the fistula, then place that fire into the hole of the fistula, and keep your hand so that the hot iron reaches the bottom of it, and at its back do this one or two times; and then treat that with what we have said about treatment so that it is healed.
And so if the pus is discharged and it is healed, that is good; and if not, then it is necessary to uncover the place and cut away the corrupted bone according to what comes into its chapter.
Chapter 30
On cauterization of teeth and softening of gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are aching, and you treat those with medicines and do not help, then place the head of the patient into your lap, and ignite the cautery in the form which will come there, so that you place the canule over the tooth and insert into it the cautery ignited quickly, and keep your hand close to the patient's mouth so that the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth where it has pervened.
And then lift up your hand, and repeat the cautery several times according to what you want.
And then the patient should fill his mouth with salt water and keep it for an hour, and then spit it out, for the aching tooth is calmed and the softened gum is strengthened and the corrupted moisture is dried up.
Chapter 31
On cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or in it is cold or red, or the treatment with medicine does not help, then cauterization in that place is according to two methods: either cauterization with an iron brand, or cauterization with fire.
Cauterization with an iron brand is that you take an iron brand and heat it in a charcoal fire, and then grab the hot end and wrap it in a cloth and place it on the end of the tooth gently until it is cold.
And then repeat that several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cloth in the fire below it, and place that over the painful tooth, and place the hot iron on top of it so that the iron reaches the cavity of the tooth.
However, cauterization with fire is that you await the needle of iron or of copper, and let it be in its center some thickness and the keenness of the fire not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cautery in the form which will come, and place that over the tooth and keep your hand so that the cautery is cold; and do this several times because the pain still continues or on another day.
And have the patient fill his mouth with good water and keep it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cauterization.
And cauterize with what you wish extreme according to what is possible to you.
And this is the form of the needle:
Chapter 32
On cauterization of hernia.
When hernia are of phlegm and cold humors and are not cured by medicines, and you wish to cure them gently, then ignite the cautery such that its smoke reaches the hernia on one side, and then place it ignited on the hernia another time, so that it reaches the bottom of the hernia.
And if the hernia is small, then place the cautery according to the quantity of the hernia, and then let it be for three days and place over it a poultice soaked in ointment so that it is heated up by what the fire has burned.
And then treat that with ointment and bandages so that it is healed.

Then treat the patient with what has been previously described so that he may heal.

Chapter 26.
Of cauterization of the stomach.
When there is a movement in the stomach and the humors multiply within it and extract from its natural place and if you continue the methods of cure without concern, then let the patient lie on his back with his arms and legs extended and cauterize him with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet according to the amount of a finger with a hot cautery, and two cauteries below the ribs so that the figure of the cauteries comes to a triangle; and separate those which are not fitted because they will make a piercing.
And if the depth of the cautery is according to the quantity of two-thirds of the thickness of the skin.

Chapter 27.
Of cauterization of a feverish liver.
When one feels in the liver pain due to chill or humidity or from grossness and it is driven from its natural place by an excessive egress of superfluous humors, and is treated with what we have said but without concern, then let the patient lie on his back with his arms raised, and sense three cauteries according to this form and quantity placed on the liver below the hypochondria where the position of a man's elbow would be.
And let the space between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cauteries be according to the length of the body towards the right.
Do not move your hand while placing the cautery.
And if the amount of that which is heated is according to the thickness of a finger's tip and there is not much heat; and if the patient stands on his feet; then let him sit, for he has already extended himself and raised his arms.

And beware that you do not apply too much in the cauterization, and let the surface of all the skin be heated so that it penetrates throughout the body and into the intestines, for here the liver is subtle; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of puncturing a boil on the liver with a cautery.
When there is a boil or ulcer on the liver and you wish to know if this boil is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver the patient will suffer grave pain and acute pain.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver then with the pain being very acute and having fatigued the doctors with his cure.
Then let the patient lie on his back, and sense the location of the boil with tentation; then let the cautery be like a needle, the form of which has been described in the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery throughout so that all the skin is heated and it penetrates into the intestines, where one would expect pus to flow.
Then treat him for the cure of other boils so that he may heal.
And do not use this form of cautery except by him who has practiced it in the art of medicine and in whose hands many illnesses have been cured; but instead experiment this once or twice on a patient, but then let them stop.
Chapter 29.
Of cauterization for a hernia.

And do not put your hand on the cautery, but rather hold it in front of you in plain view.

Chapter 30.
Of the cauterization of the ulcer.
When you cure the illness of the ulcer with what we have said about treatment in the division and do not delay your treatment, then the cautery on it is according to three methods, all of which are correct, one of which is that you make three cauteries or four depending on the length of the ulcer according to the figure of the cauteries of the liver, the figure of which has preceded.
And between each cautery, the thickness should be the thickness of a finger or a little more, and the manner of the cautery should be the same way we have described in the cautery of the liver alike.

And there is another way to cauterize, which is that you ignite the cautery having two turnings, the cause of which we have said in the chapter on dislocation of muscle, and elevate the vein which is at the end of the ulcer through which passes the jaw of the left patient.
And let your elevation be such that you elevate the vein according to the length of the body so that the cauteries are made according to the width of the body.
Then insert the two turnings, ignited, into it through which you penetrate the vein from the other side.

Chapter 31.
Of the cauterization of hydrocephalus.

And then let the cautery bleed openly so that it bleeds for a long time.
And do not evacuate the patient of curing after the cautery with that which it may be for so that two intentions are added to him and healing is encouraged by him.
The shape of the cautery with which the belly is cauterized should be key-like according to that which is above it preceding, and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped according to what precedes it.

Chapter 35.

Chapter 36.

And if the wound is sealed and the materials removed from it and remain like that for a long time, then know that it has healed completely.

Chapter .45.
On the cauterization of broken bones.
When a rupture occurs in the angles and the intestines are pushed forward out of their cavity towards the genitals, and this is how it usually starts, then you must order the patient to stop eating for the day and use healing remedies so that the rupture can descend from his belly.
Then follow the patient's back with your hands over his spine, and order him to hold his breath as you examine whether there is a hole or the intestine or hernia has appeared.
Then return it with your fingers and touch the rupture on the bone of the hip, indicating the end of which is similar to a half-circle at the upper parts of the body.
Then ignite the cautery, whose shape is as follows:
until it becomes red and blisters form. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area to prevent the intestine from coming out.
You have already opened the space between the patient' s thighs, placed a cushion under them, another servant on their legs, and another on their chest, holding their hands.
Then place the cautery on that indication with your hand holding the cautery straight, and apply it there until you reach the bone of the hip within the muscle; and repeat with another application if you do not achieve this on the first application to the bone.
And carefully observe the position of the intestine during the cauterization so that you do not burn it such that it brings death or another serious illness to the patient.
And know that when you apply the cautery and do not reach the bone, your procedure will be incomplete.
And know that the cautery for children should be thinner according to their size.
Then treat the area of the cautery for three days with wine until the scar of the fire has healed.
Then treat that with other remedies until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on his back for 40 days until the area is sealed and healed.
And know that you should regulate his diet according to the length of time it takes him to heal, so that the rupture does not come out again due to the effort of healing.
And when he wants to get up after 40 days, then administer a decently made ligature and have him wear it for another 40 days.
And he should be rested from work and the consumption of food and drink, and from violent movements, for when he uses this healing regimen entirely he will recover.
And now I will describe the healing of ruptures in other parts of the belly, and so at the beginning, and you want me to translate, then cauterize that rupture with a red hot cautery according to its quantity, and it is what has come out from the vein; then treat that as we have said, and it has not been adjusted.

and it is drawn as a line that goes through the length of the rupture, and another line above it; then place your hand with a nail-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter .46.
On the cauterization of torture.
When there is pain in some parts from bruising or impact, and the pain persists for a long time and does not yield to any medicine, then you must cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery should be performed on all parts according to its magnitude and smallness, its strength and weakness, the location of torture and pain.
So if he is healed by the first cautery, it is good; and if not, then do the cautery again; for usually those pains are caused by punctures of a member near that member, so that you must cauterize them in order to heal the patient.
Chapter .47.

The lepers sometimes receive cauterization for great benefit, and especially if the leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.

And finally, your hand with the cautery lightly on the bone until a small impression is made, and from him are displaced the scabs, so that the expiration of thick vapors is relieved from him; then cauterize over the pus according to what has been prescribed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the body and appears manifestly, then you must cauterize him with those cauteries which we have said in the head; and with a cautery on the end of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the neck, and with six cauteries on the spine of the neck, and with one large cautery over the spine near the armpit, and with a cautery on each shoulder blade, and with two cauteries on the knees, and with one on each ankle, and with two cauteries on the hips, and with one great cautery over the spine, and with two cauteries on the shins, and with two cauteries on the tendons of his arms, and with one great cautery over the tendons of his feet, and with two cauteries on all the joints of his hands and feet.
And be careful of the nerves which are upon the heel so that you do not burn those.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the bone of the hip and with another over the mouth of the stomach, and with another over the tongue.

And those who have great or few cauteries according to the parts and joints as it is preceded by the spices of the tools.

Here begins the fiftieth chapter.
Of the cauterization of warts.
When a wart begins and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a ring cautery around its circumference as it is turned.
And the wise say it should be cauterized with the last cautery in the middle of it; but I have not seen this, because I am afraid that it would be ulcerated and I have seen this happen several times.
And it is more appropriate that it is cauterized around its circumference with a ring as we have said, or with sharp cauteries.
Here begins the fifty-first chapter.
Of the cauterization of tumors.
When a tumor is delayed in its maturation, and also because of the superfluity it produces that, or because of the condition of the patient, when he is old and decrepit, or few-blooded, or because of time, and you want to hasten the maturation of the tumor; then cauterize around its circumference with small sharp cauteries; and then let it be lanced so that it will hasten in maturation.

Chapter 56.
Of cautery for the flow of blood accidentally by incision of an artery.
Sometimes there is a flow of blood from an artery that is cut through the wound, or through the incision of an abscess or cautery on the limb, and sometimes they appear similar, and it is difficult to stop that flow.
So when it happens to someone, then you should cover the orifice of the artery with your hand and place your finger on that orifice, and sometimes push that finger inside until the flow of blood is stopped and does not come out again.
Then place on the wound some large and small olive-wood coals, many in number, and blow upon them until they are very hot.
And then take some of those coals, either small or large according to the place and wound in which the artery is broken, pushing them quickly into that vein as soon as you have removed your finger, and hold them there until the blood flow is stopped and that artery is bound up and restricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the orifice of the artery when you remove your finger and tighten the cautery, then take another cautery which has been prepared in the fire, and therefore do not cease doing this one after another until the blood flow is restricted.
And be careful that you do not burn the nerves that are there, and that no other evil comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to restrict the blood in those cases except by one of four methods: either by cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it has not been extracted; for when it is extracted, its extremities are burned and the blood flow restricted; or by being tied with a thread or ligature with strong pressure; or by having remedies placed on it which have the virtue of restricting the blood flow, which should be pressed with a bandage with decent pressure.
But he who applies decent restriction to it by ligature and pressure with a bandage, or by the position of burning causes and similar things, does not confer anything but harm.
But if this happens to someone and the doctor is not present, or medicine, then open with the tip of your finger over the orifice of that vein as we have said before, and understand that it should be pressed with great pressure on him until the blood flow is stopped from the upper part of the wound; and do not remove your finger from over the artery; and sprinkle over it water which has been rubbed with something very cold until the blood is congealed and thickened; and then stop at what needs cautery or medicine.
