And insert a tampon into the womb, and over it a piece of clean wool soaked in water in which there are crushed mallows.
Then it should be treated for two days with an ointment that is appropriate for healing.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed with water in which there is a mixture of honey and water, and then with water in which there is crushed lilies or long aristolchia, and then return to using the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the womb, then you should be prohibited from treating it with iron, but instead treat it as we have described in the previous section.
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives how to treat living fetuses when they deviate from their natural shape.
It is necessary that the midwife knows the shape of the natural fetus by its description, and when you see the woman expressing herself from the lower parts and desiring to breathe the air, and being lifted up above what is on top of her, and hastening the exit of the fetus, then you will know that this position is according to nature, and it is the exit of the fetus above its head, and the second one with it or suspended or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore when you see these signs, then the belly of the fetus should be compressed so that the fetus descends gently as if it were descending over its head, and the second one with it, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with good cleansing.
But the one that is contrary to this part is not natural or praiseworthy, for when the fetus exits on its feet and its body exits before its head and its hands, or by its foot, or or its head and hand, or by its belly, and strongly turned towards its neck, and resembling the non-praiseworthy shapes.
It is necessary that the midwife be wise and subtle in all these causes, and that she guard against error and deception.
And I will show you all the figures of those, and what cunning is in them so that it may be understood by that and be placed upon that.
When the fetus exits above its head by natural effort, and it is violent upon the woman and a difficult birth for it, and sees its strength already weakened, then do that, or let the women hold it, and evaporate its moisture with fenugreek powder and with oiled warm water.
Then the midwife should insert her finger with a small sponge and split it with that finger, or dry that with her fingernail while she inserts the sponge into the opening. And press the belly of the woman so that the fetus exits.
But if it does not exit, then let the woman be cleansed with poultice of psyllium and poultice of fenugreek with honey.
And then command it, the midwife, to be compressed and make it vomit with mild medicine; and take its mouth and nose for an hour, because the fetus will exit head-first immediately.
And if both hands of the fetus have exited at the beginning, then you should reduce them with gentle compression little by little. But if it does not obey the instrument, then place the woman on her side and lift its feet up, and then rock the woman from side to side while she is held so that she does not fall due to the rocking.
And if the hands are not bent back and the fetus is dead, then insert those and pull the joint of the fetus or hold the body by its hand with a cloth and pull it equally, for it will exit.
When the fetus exits on its feet, then you should push those up, and then turn the fetus gently, and then hold one of its feet and extend those gently little by little.
But when it has exited between the thighs, then you command it to be expressed, and make it vomit with medicine, for it will exit if God wills.
And if it is not exited with what we have recommended, then return the fetus little by little to its original position, for it will exit with easier exit.
And if it is prohibited by all that we have recommended, then take the poultice of alder and dissolved gum, and beat them both in the mortar with good percussion, and apply them to the moisture of the woman, to the lowest part of her belly. Then do that cut in lukewarm water, while you obtain the hypocondiums.
And when the lower parts of it are loosened, then do to it the fumigation of myrrh and apply that to it below. And when you have done the fumigation for an hour, then do that to rest on a pillow, and then make it vomit and open its mouth, and lift up the lower part of its belly with gentle compression, for the fetus will exit immediately.
Examine the foot laterally, while one of his muscles is stretched. Let it be reduced by flexing his hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and let her go.
And if she cannot go, then let her lie on the bed and strike his thighs with a violent concussion, and administer poultices which are made of mucilaginous herbs.
And if delivery has come on, then let her lie on her back, and study the reduction of the hand, and flex the foot according to its natural shape, and make the woman sneeze and command him to press in upon himself while it is dislocated.
Examine the foot over his shoulder with his muscles expanded and his fascia stretched out behind his mother. Let the midwife take the hand of the foot and twist it little by little, and let that be facilitated.
And when it is and how much it is flexed, then pause over the navel of him that which we have recom-posed of oil and mucilaginous herbs.
And then command him to flex his hand little by little, and when you have done that, then let her lie on one side and command him to be bent over on the right side, and then command him to be pressed in; and make her sneeze with a cold. Exit of the fetus prepared on the side.
When it comes out of the mother according to this manner, consider and then insert your finger inside that it may be evacuated of moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over his face and neck, then insert that of the plum, so that it may not be retained and delayed.
And then press the fetus into the parts within, so that it may pause according to how you desire, and command the woman to press in on herself several times, and let him be dislocated with easy examination, if God wills it.
Exit of twins or many fetuses. Know that many times twins are born, and some times three or four; but that is strange and difficult to achieve; but the cause of it is nature, and they do not live.
And count those, if they are multiplied, a second one, and they are separated by the navel, distinguish those between the ligament in the navel of each of them; And some times they are dislocated so that it is an easy examination, and sometimes they exit through a non-viable examination as we have recomposed it.
And if they are difficult to deliver and reach what we have said about the non-natural shapes, then you cure them as we have recomposed it.
And if the midwife does what she does with facility and subtlety, and delay, and let it be prepared according to its shape according to how it is to him, bringing about health.
Remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and are aborted.
Sometimes they are formed in the womb in groups of one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten, and I have indeed witnessed a woman giving birth to six all formed, whose cause was made through the measurements of the glorious wise ones.
And all that while, let it be in the science of the midwife, how strong she is to that small thing, and then pause at that, with which she comes to that.
Chapter .lxxvi.
On extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you cure the dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medicine and it is not taken out, and you must use cauterization with a hot iron, then consider if the woman is of sound health and has none of the accidents by which there is feared loss over her.
Then let the woman lie on her back over her shoulder, and his head be bent down and his thighs raised up.
And the minister must hold her by two parts, or let her be tied to the bed so that his body may not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mouth of his mother with oiled waters and a poultice of alder and of fenugreek and of flaxseed.
And then the midwife takes his hand with those oils and poultices, and then flexes his hand with facility in the mother, and then places his hand upon the place where he can catch the nails of the fetus.
And convenient places for that: then you consider if the fetus is prepared to descend over his head, and then you catch the nails in the joints of him, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or in the palate, or under the beard, or in the armpits, or near the ribs, or below the hip bones.
But if the descending is over his feet, then in the instep, or in the middle of the shins.
And let him catch the nail with his right hand and pause at the bend of it in that place which is between the joints of his left hand, and flexes his hand with facility and catches the nail in any of those places which we have said, so that he may reach the cause bent.
And then pause and catch another nail, or the third one if necessary, so that it may be an attraction together, and not an extension according to its uprightness only.
And let it be flexed to all parts, so that the examination of him is drawn away as it is done in tooth extraction.
And let it be flexed between them so that the extension is softened; and if any part of him is retained, then let the midwife anoint his joints with oil and insert a poultice and twist it upon what is retained.
Then if the part of the fetus is dislocated, then let the nails be removed into that place where it was pushed out; and do that until all the fetus is dislocated.
But if the hand is extracted before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it by flexing him, then let the drape be wrapped around it; and then extract it so that all of it is dislocated, and insert it into the joint of the hand or in the wrist.
And do that you do in the other hand and in both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or there is difficulty in flexing him in examination, or he has watery swelling on his head, then let the midwife insert between the joints aspen with thorns and pierce the head and extract the water, or scrape him with an instrument which is named "scraper", the shape of which will come from the shapes of other instruments in the chapter which comes next.
And similarly, if the fetus is of a large head by nature, then cover it with a cloth, or take it out slowly as we have said; and then pull the bones out with forceps.
And if the head has come out and is stuck at the chest, then it must be cut open so that the moisture in the feet can flow out.
But if it is not complicated or obstructed, then you must push the head in immediately as soon as possible for you.
And if the bottom of the belly is ulcerated or swollen, then cover it so that you pierce where the moisture in it runs.
But if the fetus is above its feet, then the attraction of it is lighter and the direction of it towards the mother's mouth is lighter.
But if it is stuck at the belly and feet, then cover it so that you pull out that with a cloth which you will have wrapped around your hand, and cut open the belly and feet so that what is in them runs out.
But if the other members are out and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she inserts her hand in the air passage, and encloses the head with that, and pulls it in towards the mother's mouth, and then she inserts a finger or two into it and pulls it with those.
And if the mother's mouth is obstructed by an ulcer which happens to the lip, then it must not be touched there; rather it must be treated with the application of moist warm causes. And administer plasters; and follow the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then it should be turned if possible; and what we have said for the living fetus must be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it must be cut in the thighs and pulled out.
And be sure to leave no cause inside the womb.
And when you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said about the cure for ulcers which happen to the mother.
And if blood flow occurs, then use coagulants in waters and administer the other cure.
And I have also seen a woman whose belly contained a dead fetus, and then another burning fire within it, and then the other fire was the living fetus. It happened to her long ago that there was an ulcer in her navel; and it was swollen so that fire opened, and she began to give birth to arms.
And I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I placed on her some plasters of strong attraction, and a bone came out of her, and then after a few days another bone; and I was very astonished that there were bones in the belly.
And so I thought they were the bones of the dead fetus.
Then I healed the wound and pulled many bones out of her, the woman remaining in the same dispositions, and she lived for a long time after that, walking with a little limp.
And I did not consider this anything strange, but rather that in it there is knowledge and aid to what the doctor effects with his hand for cure.
On the forms of the instruments which are necessary to pull out the fetus.
Form of the wound by which the mother is opened:
This is the form of the strain where the lips are pulled, to which strain there are two virtues at both ends of two bowstrings. However this virtue must be made more subtle by compression, and it should be made either of ivory or of wood of box-tree.
And the breadth of each staff is two fingers and the thickness of each is a finger and a half; and in the middle place of the two staffs there are two settings of that staff which have already been fastened, the length of each is a palm and a half, and the breadth of each is two fingers, or a little more.
And these two settings are inserted into the mother's mouth so that it is opened by them when you turn the two said virtues.
Other similar form, but more subtle and longer:
Let it be made of ivory or of box-tree according to the likeness of forceps. However, at the end of it there are two settings like a comb, and the length of those settings is a palm, and the breadth of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with them, then let her lie on her back with her feet hanging down, opening that which is between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument just as you do with tongs, according to how much of the opening of the mother's mouth you want the midwife to do what she wants.
The form of wound which the ancients said:
Form of impulsion by which the fetus is pushed:
Form of compression by which the large head is contained:
And it is like forceps whose teeth are at the end, just as teeth of a vice with which it is opened and contained. Form of compression few:
Form of a finger having a thorn:
Form of a finger having two thorns:
Form of a spatula having two ends by which the head is cut off:
Form of two spatulas broad:
All those instruments are exercised, and when they are more numerous and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier for the operation and better for men because of its quantity.
And you should not lack those instruments, because the necessity of them is inexcusable.
On extraction of the placenta:
When the placenta is retained after childbirth, then you should command the woman to retain her breath, and then make her sneeze with force, and if it is not expelled by that, cut it out.
And if not, then you take a bowl and pierce the covering with a hole and stop that wound with herbs opening the mouth of the mother, as well as the calcium and rue and anise and camomile and dried scacade and cassia line and frankincense. All those herbs or some of them are to be soaked in water in the bowl, and stop that over fire.
And then stop a tube of reed in the mother's mouth, and hold it there while the evaporation comes to the hollow of the mother.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the placenta is expelled with speed.
But if that cure is applied and it is not expelled, then command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in mallow juice, and then insert that into the navel and probe with it the placenta, and when she has found the placenta, pull it out little by little according to how the mother does not feel strong attraction. Before anything else, ensure that the patient's right and left ribs are easily accessible.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted, we have already recommended the cure with a snuff and medicine from the oil with herbs.
But if it is not cured by all the causes which we have recommended, then beware that you do not tire yourself out with too much sucking, but rather anoint what comes out of the mouth of the mother with oil and four-drug remedy; for it will make that part soft, dissolved and cured.
But when it becomes rotten, there is a bad odour which rises to the head and stomach, and that harms the patient; therefore smoking medicines and suitable remedies should be administered to counter this.
But some of the ancients have already tried smoking with nase-tort and dry figs.
Form of an instrument by which the woman is smoked, for retention of menstruation and secundine:
It should be made of glass or brass.
And the thin end is placed in her vagina and the wider end over the fire, and the smoking is placed on the embers of the fire.
And let that remain until the smoking is consumed and then repeat another.
For cure of a non-perforated child's anus:
Young children and their anuses, if they are not perforated, become very thin and narrow. Then the midwife should enlarge it with her finger; and if not, enlarge it with a sharp spatula, and beware of touching the foreskin.
Then apply wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and cure that with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is blocked, then insert a cannula for a few days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And it often happens that the anus is blocked by a clot or an abscess; therefore the clot should be cut, and then cured with what we have said about the cure and the lead.
For cure of the fistulas that cause discharge below:
The fistulas that cause discharge near the anus, in the space of the lower part, produce a hard lump from which pus flows out.
And it is a fistula or many.
And when that lump becomes old and corroded by the putrid white or subtle pus, sometimes from some of these fistulas some penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what comes out of them by the exit and by windiness; and some have the patient of the exit, and often it comes out of them very strongly.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the bladder and into the length of the urethra; and some penetrate the joint of the thigh and into the knob of the hip.
And when you know that the penetrating fistula does not penetrate into the anus, then put your finger in the anus and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when it is soft.
But if it is hard, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsetail hair so that you feel the probe or the hair in your finger.
And if you feel nothing in it and there is no sign of excretion or windiness or anything coming out, then know that it is not penetrating, and then proceed to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the length of the urethra, then the sign of it is excretion of urine from there and prevention of it so that medicine can consolidate the place.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and there is pain in the course, then its sign is that the probe goes through there if it does not hurt in the bone, and pain in the course of the part; and there is pus from time to time; and medicine does not help it nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all penetrating fistulas there is not a cure of all, and the cure of them is difficult work of foolish physicians upon which they are settled.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected according to what you have described, and that which was seen and tried.
And it is that the patient lies on his back, and raises his legs up over him, with his thighs bent towards his belly, and then insert a probe of lead or iron into the fistula until you know the place where the probe goes through.
And if he feels that around his anus, then let him insert your finger into his anus.
And if you feel the probe already pushed in and discovered to penetrate, also what you feel between your finger and that it stretches or flesh, then know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure for that, as we have said.
But some say that it heals in some people.
And for the medicine by which it is expected to heal quickly in him is that you apply cautery subtly according to the width of the fistula, as it has been mentioned, and insert that hot cautery into the fistula until it comes out around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those stretched flesh have become dried like a reed of bird feather.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in wool until all those flesh have dried up, and then cure it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, well; but if not, then it will not be healed at any time by another cure.
And if the probe goes in but does not penetrate into your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a stretched flesh or swelling, and the fistula goes to where it pierces the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will dry it with the probe, and it will be loose in the fistula at the place where the end of the probe goes through, and by the probe it is loosened and drawn out.
And then clean those flesh which are like a reed of bird feather, and none of the causes remain.
But if blood flows from between you and your probe when you push in those flesh, then the milk that is and of the pus the sharp instrument is cauterized with fire or with sharp medicine, for cautery with fire tightens good flesh: so it is drying of those stretched flesh, and abscission of the blood the drying of the moisture.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in wool or in cress within which the place is hot, and then cure the wound with hard flesh-generating and consolidating ointments.
And thus medicines in which there is constriction and drying; when then it is restored, then it is healed and does not fear repetition.
But if the fistula already goes through into the foreskin of the anus and is elongated from the superficial part of the body, then insert your finger in the anus and probe it.
And so if you feel resistance and between you and it is veil of membrane or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation on him but according to the way of confidence and hope.
This can be treated in one of three ways: cauterize it as we have said; cut away the skin around the wound and try to find the cavity so that you can enter with your fingers and apply treatment directly; do not cut deeply enough to damage the membrane covering the spine, and let the patient pass wind freely according to his will, so that the swelling in the anus will go down of itself. Then treat it as we have said before, so that it will heal quickly; or enlarge the membrane and open the pustules with a sharp instrument as you cut away the skin around the wound, or with a probe, and clean out the fistulous sores which you find inside by probing; try your best to cure the whole wound by cutting, and if the orifice above is not healed over, and the fistula still drains from near the anus, then it will heal more quickly. And sometimes the fistula is cured in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, it is near the edge of the anus, on the surface of the body, and around the margin of the anus; then take a probe with a pointed end, like a needle for piercing ears, and prepare it in this way:
and insert it into that fistula, wrapped in a piece of wool or linen, and then insert the probe into the fistula until you find its cavity.
But if you feel resistance near the edge of the anus inside, then withdraw the probe from that wound; extract the end of the probe and tie the two ends together, and leave it in place for a day or two; and when you have pierced the membrane with the probe, tighten it around the piercing so that the flesh between the two ends of the probe is squeezed.
Then cure the wound until it is healed and consolidated.
But if the orifice of the fistula is not penetrated, then make it so, in whatever way you are skilled, unless it damages the membrane, for you must not pierce the membrane; and then cure it until it is healed.
The shape of the spatula with which the fistula is split: one end is very sharp and the other blunt, so that you do not pierce what is unnecessary, as we have said.
About stitching up hemorrhoids from which blood flows when they are cut.
There are two types of hemorrhoids: some appear inside the anus like a bunch of grapes, and others look like a bunch of raisins. They are small or large; the blood flows from all of them at some time; they are inside and outside the anus, and at its edges.
But those which are outside the anus are according to the season of less moisture; from these flows water like yellow bile or a little blood with continuous pus; they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you order the patient to sit down and squeeze it until the anus protrudes, so that the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grab it and hold it with your fingernail or something else which you have; then cut off those roots.
But if the fingernails are not held in them because of their moisture and softness, then take them with rough cloth or a twig or woolen thread, and extract them with your teeth, and then pierce them and sprinkle some powdered herbs on the wound at the point of piercing, so that they may serve instead of cauterization; or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cauterization. Then cure them with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey excretion, then cut the patient's anus with a sharp instrument so that it has a small bite; and let him be washed with water which is in the anus until he excretes quickly when he is squeezed.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is looser; take them with your fingernail or something else which you have, and pierce them, and cure them as we have said until they are healed.
And if you see the cut, know that you should give them sutures in this way: take a piece of wool and insert it into the needle, and then extract the hemorrhoids at their tops; then make the needle penetrate the root on the other side, and wrap the two ends of the wool around the needle below the needle, and tie them latitudinally; then tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong grip, and withdraw the needle.
And do this to all of them; cut one and not the others, so that the surplus blood flows from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerot over the anus, and let him rest. And then let it remain until it is healed; when it is healed, cure them with ointment or some other cure until they are completely healed.
And if they are old and the medicine does not help, then know that you should pierce them with the point of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist, and let the suppuration occur above them, which prevents their consolidation; then cure them until they are consolidated.
Then you should stop those with a more violent bleeding than the first, until they no longer bleed and are excoriated; then cure them until they are completely healed.
Know that ulcers, which are key, and do not if the body is not red around the color of a nail head; they occur throughout the body and mostly in the lower parts, the feet and the fingers, and through these there is pain.
Then know that it should be pierced where there is a circle around the nail, and held with forceps or something else which you have, and pulled out by its root; then cure it.
And if you wish, cauterize it as described in the previous chapter.
But warts are small rough round pustules on the surface of the body; they often occur in children's hands.
And its treatment is that you stretch and pierce it, or squeeze it with a needle until it pops out. And if you wish, cauterize it as we have said, with fire or sharp medicine.
But a carbuncle is a large pustule on the surface of the body which occurs in the armpits; when it is rubbed, it feels like an insect bite, and it occurs around the body.
And its treatment is to take a cane of reed or of peacock's feather, and make it strong; then place it over the carbuncle until it contains the circle of the cane; then turn your hand around the cane so that you pierce what is in the circle of the carbuncle, and let him squeeze the canule so that he pulls out the root of the carbuncle.
And if you want, make this with a tube of gold or iron in this way:
Let the part above the cannula be thin, smooth (so that it is not perforated), and wrapped so that over the said part the perception of it is abolished and the revolution of that.
And if you want to cauterize the ant according to what is mentioned above in the cautery of atheyl in the chapter on cauteries.
On the shape of the instruments that are administered to give surgery.
Sometimes surgery is done with silver or brass or molten gold or malleable; and sometimes they are made of those instruments small and large according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the cure of small children should be small; and of those whose anus is sore or painful, the cristiers of these should be small.
The shape of a large and few cristiere:
The head of the cannula above which is tied the pouch should be wide in this way; and there should be a distinction to it where the pouch is tied as well as the veins.
And the extremity of it below, which is inserted into the anus, should be smooth (so that it is not open) very little, preventing subtlety; and on one side of it there are two holes, and on the other side a hole, and the width of the hole according to the thickness of the rays, and the thicker ones smaller.
And the pouch in which are placed the medicines of a beast's bladder or sheep's wool, and its length be one palm.
And it should be perforated with many holes as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes, the thickness of the said instrument.
And then insert into those holes strong bile of raw bile which should be from twelve biles, or surround them with it; and when it is tied to him, the pouch, let it be tightly tied at the head of the cristiere between two distinctions with a strong constriction, and then cauterize with that each hole: But the quality of cauterizing with that is that you fill the pouch with medicine, which should be warmed according to the quantity of the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not too cold or too hot.
And then the patient should lie on his back, and raise his legs up and place under him a board upon which there are men or a lanceole; and then anoint the extremity of the cautery with oil or with albumen of egg or with mucilage of fenugreek or of similar things; and then insert that into the anus with ease, and let the minister hold the pouch and squeeze it with two hands and press with force until everything within the cristiere is evacuated; and then remove the cristiere and the patient raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces that to come out, let it be as much as possible; and if he sleeps with that for a whole night, it is better for healing.
But if you cauterize that in the diseases of a loose belly and its discharge, then repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the cristiere be very thin and smooth, of those cristeres with which children are cauterized.
On the cure of wounds.
You have already said in the division of the cure of wounds what suited the free one, and I say thus the cure of those with hand and medicines according to the complement.
Therefore I say that wounds are varied according to what they are made of and according to the place on which they fell.
And the causes for which many times wounds are made are thus with a stone, or incision with a knife or dagger, or blow with a spear, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar things.
But the wounds that are in the parts of the body are thus with a wound which falls on the head, or neck, or feet, or belly, or thigh, and similar things to those of the members.
And I am recommending the cure for some wounds so that you pause that reasoning and rule over the other wounds.
And I will begin with the simple wounds of the head, for the recollection of the compound ones will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when it happens in the head a simple wound and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is with a stone or similar, and the tendon has only just been cut and it is a large wound and you fear an abscess on the patient, then you should apply a cautery to the wound and a fleubotomy to the temporal artery immediately, and not delay that; and remove from there blood according to the quantity of its virtue if no other cause prohibits fleubotomy, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is little.
And let the fleubotomy be on the side opposite the affected part; and keep the patient from replection and wine and coarse foods.
But if some cause prohibits it, then let him be lessened of food but not approach meat or wine.
And place over the wound, if it happens to him an abscess, a hot poultice in rose oil or wine in which is mixed.
But if he is certain of an abscess, then place over the wound if it is fresh with his own blood and the air has not changed that which I recommended earlier, then place over it, then small.
And if the air has already changed that, then place over the wound albus poultices until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or it is by incision with a knife or similar, and its lips are not drawn together by threads, then draw those together with sutures according to what I recommended for them.
But if the wound is uncovered of bone and pen, and there is no retention of it except for a small suspensory, then insert that and then cure the wound with medicines and restore the flesh by cutting in the place of tendon, and command the patient to abstain from foods in which there is coarseness: garlic and formen cow and similar things to them, until it is healed.
And if you see in the tendon putrefaction and it does not adhere to bone, then insert everything rotten and cut that, and then cure that.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow and it is not stopped by medicines, then tie over the artery, and if you find that uncut, then insert that with spathum, or ligature that; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said, then cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is coldness; for this you powder over those this powder, for when that has dried in front, the air changes it.
And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of aloes one part, dragon' blood two parts, and causticum not-extinguished three parts; let all be ground and sifted, and let the wound be filled with it, and let it be compressed with a good constriction so that it lies upon it to harden, and let the blood coagulate.
And then tighten that above with a bandage and let it be left so that it is not loose while the wound is unhealed, for otherwise it will not be healed.
And sometimes it does this on its own, when you are not in the presence of dragon's blood.
And this treatment is also performed on large wounds when it is appropriate to apply it directly and the wound is fresh with blood, which has not been altered by air.
And if the air has altered them and especially in summer, then there is no intention to use this powder, but instead cure them with what we have said, that you apply some astringent ointments or paste made from barley flour mixed with water and honey; and also cure them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if there is a broken bone in the wound and it is small, then you must remove it with tweezers.
And I will describe the cure for fractures of the head when it has been poisoned.
Of wounds in the neck:
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment and it is the same as for a simple wound on the head.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or an artery, then if it is a nerve, there should be no irritation but rather avoided by applying something that causes strong constriction, as well as cinchona and a kind of vitriol which is harmful to nerves and acts as a counterirritant. And you must not apply anything hot to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is sensitive and its continuation is to the brain, which is the source of all the members.
And if it is treated with mild medicines, as well as causing washing with warm water, some poultice soaked in rose oil or not, and cauterization when it is washed with warm water and the other stones are removed according to this manner, and ointments moistened.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures or cut the lips of it, and then cure it with powders until it is healed.
But if there is a deep wound or an infection in the lower part, then pierce it in the lowest place.
But if an artery is cut in the wound and blood flow is achieved, then stop or dry it, or ligature it, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the windpipe or throat is completely cut through and the veins of the neck are preserved, then you must sew up the edges of the wound on the windpipe without touching it; raise it up and return it to its natural shape, and then bind it tightly with a decent bandage, and let that be for several days; and then cure it with ointments in which there is constriction and excitation until it is healed.
But if the wound has been made by pressure on bone, then pierce it and if there are fragments of bone in it, extract all that you can; and what you cannot, let that be within so that the wound becomes pustular, and let the discharge of it be separated and the swelling around it be relieved.
And if the wound is fresh, then powder it with the powder which I have recommended to you. But if a fistula is made in the wound, then cure it with that which its memory will come to you soon in this chapter.
If it is a thrust of a lance or spear and you see the depth of it, then consider that the wound is deadly if the victim shows shortness of breath; then know that the wound is lethal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then you must apply powder to it in the first hour and not constrict it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of it and returns to the heart and that the victim is relieved, but rather apply attracting ointment to it, and loose it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not present to you, then you must place a sharp instrument in the wound's opening so that it sucks out what is in the humid parts; and let the victim sleep with their head over the wound so that what is inside it flows out.
But if three days or more have passed and there are no signs of spasms or trembling of the heart or stricture of the chest, and you see the other good dispositions of it, then know that the wound is healed; and then cure it with poultices and with the other treatment until it is completely healed.
But if its healing is delayed and all time it remains open, then know that a fistula has been made; cure it therefore according to its chapter.
And if the wound is made by a spear or lance and is simple on the surface of the foot or leg, then cure it with that which precedes sutures if it is large, or powder if it is small.
But if it has made an impression on the bone and the fragments are separated from it, then you must twist the wound and cut to extract those fragments if they are separate. But if they are not separated, then let them be within so that the wound becomes pustular and the extraction of them is relieved.
And for other wounds by accidents on other members, the treatment in cure is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds in the abdomen:
The disposition which occurs in the abdomen is either large or small or punctured.
And a small disruption is made difficult in another way and you see that their reduction is more difficult if you do not cut off the intrusion of them to their place at once, for it opens up and becomes swollen so that the insertion of it is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is punctured because in it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult as is difficult in the two species mentioned above.
And know that the suture of the abdomen is according to four ways, but two sutures are common in sutures of other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to other wounds but rather are more proper in the suture of the abdomen.
And I will recommend to you each one with exposition and explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and there is some cause of the intestine through it, and its reduction is made difficult, then it is in one of two ways: or because of the smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when the intestine receives cold air.
And therefore when that is so, you see that you warm it with a sponge or cloth moistened in only tepid water, or that you place it in water made warm, and cyperum, and spic, and embrocations on the intestine so that the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it does this with the substance which has constriction, and is better than just water in resolving inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation is resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which there is a little vinegar and mallows, for by this it is relieved the substance of it with little cause.
But if the instrument is not suitable for this cure, then split the hemorrhoids with a small instrument with which they are cut.
And the first cure is better when it is not a cut, and there is no occupation to dry by necessity and deflation.
Of the instrument, see its form:
One part of it is acute and bent, and the other part not acute, and the end fine, and not be as fine as a spatula, but rather blunt a little.
And therefore when the wound is enlarged and the intestine comes out, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form, in its proper place, if possible on it, for it is better.
But if the hemorrhoid is large and is in the lower part of the belly, then you should make the patient lie on his back, and place his legs higher than his head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you should place his head and chest higher than his legs.
And likewise if the hemorrhoid is in one of the two parts of the belly, then pay your attention and your consideration equally to both so that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this for large hemorrhoids and for smaller hemorrhoids.
And for small hemorrhoids, use what is prepared for you, and make the minister hold it between his fingers, which have the hemorrhoid entirely in his hand and press its lips together, and uncover from that to that one side of the stitch, any cause whatever.
And this is the way of common sewing: one that you take the needle, or count of needle according to the amount of the width of the wound; and let go of the end of the hemorrhoid of the little finger, and strengthen the needle one, and do not put oil in both ends of the thread, with two ends of the ligature which is under the hemorrhoid of the fingers within, so that you make it penetrate from that party; and you have already added two ends of the thread and two ends of the ligature, and so are made four orders.
And strengthen with double oil around the needle, and several times from two parts together, and add both lips and the hemorrhoid by a decent addition; and let go of the thickness of the finger and put another needle in that one, and tie it up thus with oil as it was with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if it is necessary with needles until you have completed all the contraction of the wound.
And let the needles be between coarseness and fineness, for fine needles pierce gently into the flesh; and coarse ones are of difficult insertion in the thread, and therefore so that they be finer in fineness and coarseness.
And it is not that the needle be fixed in both ends of the thread next to each other, so that the flesh is not pierced gently and that the wound opens before its solidification.
And they are not prolonged with sewing so that the wound is prohibited from solidification.
And pierce the ends of the needles so that they do not hurt the patient when he sleeps; and you will place on the wound powdered expanders of all parts, which have the ends of the needles; and let it be until you know that the wound is solidified.
And this kind of sewing with needles in any way is more convenient for small hemorrhoids, for it has in their sewing one needle or two or around them.
But the second sewing is common: and it is that you sew the four ends, of the thread and two ends of the ligature, in one go, with a needle in which there is oil wrapped both in fineness and coarseness.
And when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, reduce the needle from that meted part from which you began, so that the oil withdrawn box of the most sublimated part to the wound, so that the sewing is according to sewing of cloth with which the cloths are tightened.
And place between each sewing the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this sewing is known to men.
And with such sewing I healed the wound which was inflicted on a man in his belly, and it was wounded by a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and he had come out of the intestines around two palms from the intestinal membrane, and the rupture was in the middle part of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been out of the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was solidified in the space of 15 days or around it, and I healed him and he is healthy; and thus he lived for many years, and is in his senses.
And other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle he was healed, for I did not cure him with ointment, but placed on the wound a piece of wool two times a day and an frequent ablution with water mixed with honey until he was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of sewing according to the account of the Word.
And it is that you begin with sewing of the thread, and pierce the needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And therefore when the needle penetrates in the thread and in that you pierce the rectum and in length of all the body, and let go of the end of the ligature in which you have pierced the needle; and make it penetrate from the other end from within to without in the other end of the anus.
And when you have made that penetrate, then you make it penetrate another time in the meted end of the anus from within to without in the other end of the anus; and let go of the end of the ligature which is in this side, and make the needle penetrate in the other end of the anus.
And make it penetrate with that your penetration with which you make it penetrate in the anus in the end of the anus which is in the part of him so that all that penetrates.
And begin from this meted side and sew that with the end which is of the ligature in the other side, and pull out that needle from the thread which has penetrated, and then reduce it in that thread and tie the end of the ligature which is in the other side with this end of the anus; and pull out that needle from the thread which is of part of him.
And do that one time and another, and do that one time about another time so that you sew all that is out according to that example.
And the sum of this way of sewing is that you sew with sewing of skin with which you sew the ligature, assuming that you sew the anus one time from the end of the thread, and let it be until you have completed it.
But the second kind of sewing proper which I say is from the word of Galienus, and this is of semen of him and by him composed, is that you sew it according to similarity with which they compose medicine, assuming that you add all part to its compared part to him conveniently by nature; and apply one end of him to the other end of him, and this is done according that I have composed.
Place the needle at the tip of the eye that is closest to the side of the wound, and make it penetrate into the inside of the wound in that sun, and tie the two ends of the thread; then twist the needle and make it pass through from the outside to the inside in the two ends of the ciphr.
Then twist those knots and make some of the outside of the wound pass inside into the inside of the wound in the two ends of the eye that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than common suture, and it is a suture of which I have given a description.
This is the speech of Walga
And some say that the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles; therefore you must put a needle into one of these, and begin with the insertion of the needle from what is below you, and make it pass into the other side according to the suture of those who sew skin to skin.
And know that when there is a disruption in the middle of the belly, then the suture of it is more difficult than that of the other places in the belly.
But the judgment of medicine for plague is the judgment of the other plagues.
And the cause is that when you encounter the plague recently with its blood before the air alters it, and return the intestine and cook it and arrange it decently, then place over it a carnative powder.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then place over it certain unction which are to you present that they make hard stone and draw out the puss, and be consolidated thread and eye; and cure that thus as is the cure of the other plagues so that it may be healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then place over it immediately, for as soon as it begins to give off a stench, change that twice a day, morning and evening, ayssi how you are taught, until it is healed, for you do not need any other medicine in various dispositions when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague will communicate injury to the principal members, then take a lion’s wool and soak it in oil of suitable quality, or in roasted oil, and place that in the circuit of the places that are between the angles and below the armpits.
And if there is pain or putrefaction in his intestines, as many times as that happens, anoint that with black styptic wine of tebe, and especially if the putrefaction is come into the intestine and makes an exit piercing into its cavity.
And know that that which is of the gross intestines is easier to heal than that which is in the subtle intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine which is called "gegunu" does not receive healing of the plague that occurs in it at all; and that is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it, and because of the magnitude of it that is in those, and because of the subtlety of the body of that, and because of its proximity to nature of nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the plague is the fabric, and you accomplish that recently, return it according to your rectitude by which you return intestines to health.
But if it has passed to him space and is already made black or dark, then that you bind that with thread over the place which is blackened by it, in order that there come flow of blood, for in the fabric there are veins and arteries.
And then insert what is under that ligament, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from below the plague hole over you, in order that upon you be alleviated the attraction of him due to the sinking of the fabric and emission of the stench of the plague.
Description of the plagues when they happen in the intestines.
When disruption happens in the intestines and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in certain men, because he had seen a man who was plagued in his belly by a spear thrust and the plague was on the right side of his stomach, and the plague was old and made a fistula, and it was driven out from him and ventositat.
Then he pursued the cure of him until it was not believed that he could be healed, but I continued to treat him gently until the fire healed and the place was consolidated.
And he had seen the place already consolidated, he guided him about the patient that in his belly was to have an accident of evil, and did not take on any accident due to the evil and remained in his usual dispositions eating and drinking, and then bathed and exercised himself.
And some experienced men say that when the plague comes in the intestines and is small, then it should be bound according to this manner: and it is that you take foments of great caps; and then adjust the two lips of the plague and place a foment of those which has the open mouth over the two lips of the plague; therefore when he takes hold of those and tightens his mouth over them, cut off the cap from him, for it will adhere and not dissolve.
And then place another foment near the first one, and do not cease that which you do with foments, one after another according to the quantity of all the plague.
And then return that and cook the plague; and let those caps remain in the intestines until the intestines are healed; and may not happen to the patient any harm.
And it is possible that the intestines be bound with thread subtle which is made of the intestines of beasts annexed to it, for it is more in a needle; and take the end of those intestinal threads, and let there be some flesh removed from it, and then bind the end of it with thread, wound up, and then insert that thread into needles and cook it with that the intestines, and return that into the disruption.
And this manner of suture with foments is not anything other than according to way of confidence and hope.

And if you are compelled to evacuate the blood again and there is no excuse, then bleed them in that place or on the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "the strings of the arm" is bled in the place of the basilic or the cephalic vein when they are not found or are hidden, for it is composed of these.
And the quality of phlebotomy is that the patient immerses their hand in hot water so that the vein becomes prominent and appears evidently.
Then it should be bound tightly with a bandage using a small needle; and phlebotomy should be done according to the small transverse, not longitudinally nor according to length.
And let the wound be wide and your phlebotomy of it on the joint of the hand be small.
And if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse the hand in hot water again and let the blood run into the water until you have satisfied your need.
But if you are in the summer days, then you are excused from immersing your hand in hot water; and the body of the blood is bathed in hot water during the winter months.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, for there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
And phlebotomy of the outer part of the right hand is related to diseases of the heart.
And the quality of phlebotomy is that you bind the hand with a bandage or your own hand, after having immersed it in hot water, until the vein is swollen and this is demonstrated by the pulsation; then bleed it transversely; and if you insert the needle everywhere, it will not harm anything.
And beware of not penetrating your hand with the phlebotomy, for beneath are the nerves of the fingers stripped of flesh.
Then immerse the hand in hot water and let the blood flow into it, for if you do not immerse it in hot water, the blood will coagulate in the orifice of the vein and flow is prohibited.
And when the blood is drawn according to the amount of need, then place olive oil and salt over the vein so that it is not dried up harshly; likewise let them apply it in every limb.
But phlebotomy of this vein of the left hand is related to diseases of the uterus.
And do likewise in phlebotomy like you have done in the other.
And phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is related to diseases which are below the body, like diseases of the womb, and retention of menstruation, and also to diseases of the knees and old ulcers of the feet and hands, and their likes.
And the quality of phlebotomy is that the patient immerses their foot in hot water and rubs it so that the veins appear; and tie with a bandage around the ankle; and the location of the vein appears at the tip of the toe, and from there it branches into the fascia of the foot in many branches.
Then phlebotomy is performed on the broadest branch below the ankle, for it is better and safer.
And if you bleed it in the fascia of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are below it on the fascia of the foot; and phlebotomy is performed according to the way you want to insert it, as if you were to pierce it.
And let the phlebotomies be mild.
If there is a prohibition on the movement of blood, then you should raise the patient's leg in warm water and allow the blood to flow into that area which needs to be evacuated.
And if the bloodletting instrument fails on the first attempt, then repeat the bloodletting a few times, because the site is salty; and do not fear evil on him, but rather beware of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly, do this to the other foot in the same way.
And from the sciatic vein, the site is as we have said, under the talus bone below the ankle of the wild forest.
And the benefit of the bloodletting for him is at the hip joint when it is due to stagnant blood.
And the quality of the bloodletting for him is that the patient should enter a bath, and quickly and tightly tie the bandage on his thigh on the side of the wide space above the talus above four inches in one long slender bandage, because it does not appear except through that.
And so when it appears, then you should bloodlet that according to your ability and according to the cross-section, and it is better; or pierce that with a piercing instrument or cut that with a cutting tool, because the site of it is salty.
And it is in most men hidden; and if you find that, and it does not appear to the touch of all, then bloodlet one of its branches, and it is that which appears on the back of the foot opposite the little finger and ring finger.
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow in the quantity you want.
Then untie the constriction and place something on the site of the bloodletting, and tightly bind the area, because usually it heals quickly.
Of ventilation and the qualities of using those.
Ventozes are from horns, or ram's horn, or glass.
And ventilation is done according to two methods: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventilation without scarification.
And this ventilation that is without scarification is according to two methods, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the vents that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are twelve sites on the body, one of which is where a paunch is placed in the back of the head; and the middle part of the two parts of the neck together; and vents from the chin below the jawbone and below the mouth; and vents from two spatulas; and vents from alohoster, which is above the end of the tailbone; and vents from two little holes, which are in the armpits; and vents from two little bones; and vents from two thighs.
And ventilation does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it has the virtue through them as is evident through phlebotomy; nor do I think that ventilation should be done with fire or without fire in any disease caused by a full body evacuation.
But if need demands ventilation for disease or for continence, we should administer it throughout the month and in its middle and also whenever necessary.
And therefore when you see any of those causes, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we command ventilation to be done after two or three hours have passed of the day.
And the benefit of ventilation at the nape is that when it confers heaviness to the head and to that which is sunken in the eyes; but we beware that this is not a full body evacuation.
And this ventilation is at the site of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
It is not advisable to use it on anyone who has a complex fever or in whom there is catarrh, because it causes great harm to him.
And therefore we beware of using it on the elderly and on heads in which there are cold diseases.
But if after that is continued over him there is forgetfulness; and therefore we command the ventilator to pause his hand for a moment with the ventouse at the sites below, fearing the occurrence of forgetfulness.
But ventilation of alkehel is at the site of phlebotomy of the dark vein and of the basilica: and therefore it confers asthma, and to constriction of throat and to splitting of throat, and to cough, and to congestion.
And let the alkehel ventilation be stopped for a moment, because if it is stopped below, it generates weakness in the body and stomach.
And ventilation of alhagdahan confers accidental pains in the head and to ophthalmia, and hemorrhoids, and pre-vision, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is at the site of phlebotomy of the basilica.
And let us command the ventilator not to press his hand while scarifying, lest he pierce an artery and cause an outbreak of blood.
And ventilation below the chin confers colic in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and appearance to them of diseases that are in the mouth, and it stands at the site of phlebotomy of alicric, which is on the two lips.
But ventilation that is done over the two spatulas confers headaches, which are caused by congestion of heat.
And ventilation of the two sites in the pits of the armpits confers benefit from phlebotomy of three veins, namely the basilica, the cephalic and the dark; because it draws blood from all those small veins in the flesh, and those small veins from the other large veins through which there comes attraction to the three veins.
And let us command the ventilator not to go too deep in scarification, because the site is deprived of flesh, and below it are nerves and arteries.
And it is a ventilation in which it is ventilated over the buttocks and confers benefit to the hemorrhoids of the anus and to ulcers below them.
And let us command the ventilator that it be a strong ventilation, and that it is of ram's horn, because the site needs a strong section; and they pierce very small vents with a pointed cautery instrument and it is scarified with scarification.
But vents of the legs are lessened for manifest diminishment, because they draw blood from all the body, and confers benefit to ancient pains of the knees and of the uterus, and induce menstruation, and confer benefit to hernia and hemorrhoids; and they stand at the site of phlebotomy of two sophens, and in the armpit, and of two little bones.
However, they weaken all the body and cause syncope in many people.
And the benefit of vents at the armpit is that it is close to the benefits of vents at the two legs.
And the quality of the position of the leeches is that you place the leech at the beginning of the vein and suck gently, and do not prolong the position of the leeches, but remove them lightly, and keep the patient still so that the humors come to the affected area naturally. And do not stop repeating this until you see the area turn red and swell, and a heat from the blood appear.
And then you should scarify and continue to suck gently, gradually, while observing the condition of the body.
Indeed, for a person with tender skin and few scars, you should scarify once and not again so that the area does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the leech to deepen the scarification and take a little more blood, tempering the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should scarify twice: by opening up the pathway to the blood and moisture in the first scarification, you ensure that these are drawn out. But in the second, you seek to extract the thick blood itself.
And if the blood is very foul, then a third scarification should be made so that all impurities are expelled.
Finally, when we want to extract a little blood or a small amount, one scarification is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we should make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then we should scarify deeply with a wide scarification.
And the term "scarification" refers to both the depth of the cut and the width.
And if the person has a thick, tough hide with tight pores, then the areas where leeches are to be placed should be made smooth and open; in summer, this can be done using oil of aloes, oil of violets, oil of bitter almonds, or oil of camphor gum; and in winter, oil of nardus, oil of lily, oil of camomile, or blood, and similar substances.
And if the superficial layer is thick and tough, then the oils should be of myrrh, or oil of balsamita, oil of aloes, oil of aniseed, and similar substances.
But if the leech has wide pores and a tender skin, then oil should be prohibited; instead, the areas where the leeches are to be placed should be washed with hot water or hot salt water, or with water of vinegar, or with water of soap, or with similar substances.
And if the person has blood that is very moist, then wash the leech-bites with sour wine, or with water of myrtle and sumach, or similar substances.
And for those whose superficial layers are thick, wash the leech-bites with old wine, or with water of sage, which is marjoram, or with a decoction of aniseed, or of camomile, and similar substances.
And the leech-bite should be kept dry; some say that it should be exposed to the sun for an hour or two, and then the patient should sleep with the leech in place.
What is the purpose of this, that one should be bled before being leeched?
Consider the beginning: if the leech or the person to be bled is choleric and has hot blood and inflammation, then they should take cooling remedies, such as acetic milk-vetch, and endive in sour wine and lettuce, and henna, and julep, and similar substances.

And on the day of leeching and bleeding, let his drink be greater than his food.

But the leech-bites that are to be scarified should be those that are placed over the liver, and over the melsa and podagra, and over the umbilical region, in the places of the joints, and the tip of the elbow, because these members do not endure scarification upon themselves.
And it is not desired for those but attraction of blood from the member, so that they place the leech over the breast in malacia of flow of blood of the blood of the veins; and they use it so that the thick leech is dissolved and softened within the member, so that they place it over the belly and the umbilical region; because it softens the member and opens it and reduces its pain as it resolves the leech.
And indeed it is placed over the bones when they become swollen in their joint or broken, for by virtue of strong attraction it opens their joint and removes the break from its place.
And similarly, when it is placed over the liver and the melsa for those who are confined.
And these leeches should be applied with suction only; and some are applied with fire.

It is made of ramie, or citronwood, of thick margins, hemp, and thistle, in order that it does not irritate the member, it should be placed over him in its position upon him.

Thus begins the section of the third part: on restoration. This chapter is very necessary many times in the art of medicine; it is a restoration of fractures and accidental displacement in the bones.
Oh son, understand that the foolish doctors and the common people and those who have not read the books of the Ancients nor have understood a letter from them boast about this chapter.
And for this cause the manner of those learned in our land is private; and I have not been obliged to it at any time because of all; and I have not applied what I have learned, except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study on the understanding of those, until I extracted their science.
And I also add to it experience and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science tells me and about which my experience has passed; because I have shortened it for you others and freed you from occupation of elongation; and I have shortened it in the end of shortening, and shown it at the end of demonstration, and formed many forms of instruments that are administered in it, as is done through addition of demonstration, as I have done in two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
Summary and collections from being fractures of bones, from which whose precedent is necessary before we begin the remembrance of fractured members and various if any. And so that we may begin at the beginning of this chapter the summaries of speech, and the chapters to understanding of which in the beginning the artificers and in station on the truth of those are compelled; you and those who are prompt to know this noble art others from you.
And so I say that when accidents happen to any fracture or displacement or twisting or hernia, then you should begin at the beginning with phlebotomy or leeching him, or whatever poultice, unless some cause prohibits it, as is debility of strength, or he to whom it happens is an infant, or old in decrepitude, or there is violent heat, or violent rubbing.
And let him be content with food of herbs fresh and of meats of birds and of crabs; and forbid thick meats and full of food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of suppuration of cauterisation, and if you do not fear effusion of matter into the member.
And then return the patient to his first regime with which he used to cure himself.
And so when the bone is broken begins to be restored, then let the patient be fed with nourishing foods of food thick and heavy in which there is stickiness, as are foods made of wheat cooked in water, and rice, and caps, and peas, and bellies of cows, and hives, and fresh fish, and wine thick and similar things to those, and at this regime coagulation of fracture is made sooner and better.
And know that the broken bones when they are in old people or in those who are weak, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature by the drying of their members.
But nature adjusts and binds on the broken bone of all parts any cause like glue in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens together, until they come to an end of strength and firmess, as fire at the beginning, so that no cause prohibits it from its works.
And for this cause it is necessary that you stop the foods of the patient, foods in which there is heaviness and stickiness and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of members, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the toe; and similarly all other members are diverse among them.
And I have come by remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in its distinct chapter from the others.
Great diversity of fracture in itself. And the kinds of fractures are diverse in their joints; for there is made arcuated fracture another that occurs in them stars; and some times fracture according to the length of the bone: and so fractures of the satagia and asthlas and additional joints and non-additional joints, as is made in the pen when it is prepared to write, and fracture is fissure small.
And every species has its own pain or torment in its restoration according to what will come by remembrance of it distinct in its places.
A sign of bone fracture. And by this it is known that there is a fracture: if the bone feels crooked and has a protuberance which can be felt and seen, and when you press on the bone, it hurts.
And if in the spot there is no crookedness visible nor any protuberance and the patient feels no great pain when you touch it and move the bone, then here there is no fracture.
It is possible that there might be a crookedness or a small crack, then you need not worry about this if you touch it with your hand and compress it; you should put on the medicine of which you have experience at this moment when it suits the spot; and it should also be tight with a subtle bandage.
And know that when the spot is arched and not split in two or anything else which does not happen there, like when it is completely healed all over its surface, then you should start to treat it by cauterization until it is cured.
And if an ulcer occurs there, then leave it for several days until the ulcer stops; and then heal it in any way you can using medicine or by other means.
And know that the restoration and piercing of this spot is more painful than the bone in which the fracture occurred.
And then strengthen it according to what you remember about it.
But if the bone is healed, then extension of this broken member into two parts is necessary, whether it is the arm or leg; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and the hand.
And then you should place the member in its natural position as much as possible while the broken parts are extended, and you should pierce these joints with a pointed instrument or by any other means that you can think of for their location.
And beware that you do not cause pain to the patient or harm through your operation; and see to it according to your ability that you apply one bone to its fellow according to their best forms.
And you should then advantageously touch it with your hand and if you see anything else there, rectify it and heal it by the amount of your skill.
And avoid violent extension and compression, as many foolish doctors make ulcers occur through their operation or cause contraction in the member just as we have seen it through the operations of many times.
And the quality of the broken member's tightness is such that you count it; for you should know that the broken members are different in their smallness and in their greatness and in the forms of those.
And those which are small like the arm and the finger and the wrist and their likes, then you should have a very thin cloth wrap it up.
But those which are large like the thigh or leg, then in those you should have a hard wrap, because the tight wrapping connects the large member and tightens it all around with an even binding so that no crack occurs within it.
And then you should begin by adjusting and tying it, pausing with a convenient bandage over the spot of that fracture with three or four wrappings depending on what the member deserves; and tighten your hand a little with the binding.
And then you should go to that at the top of the spot of the fracture and tighten it so that your tightening does not focus on the spot of the fracture. And then you should lengthen it by wrapping the spot of the fracture a little and loosen the tightness little by little while taking a large grip on that spot.
And then you should take another bandage and wrap that other part over the broken spot, and then you should go to the wrappings at the bottom of the fracture; and your operation should be in loosening the wrapping and in softening it according to what we said above about the first wrapping.
And then you should pause between the wrappings of cotton or cloth by which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; but if not, then don't pause for any reason.
And then you should pause on it another bandage and then heal it on these decently made layers, if there is no inflammation or ulcer in that spot, and then you should pause over it whatever causes the ulcer and which stops the inflammation; and let that be for several days; and then you should tighten the layers.
And the layers should be made of thin split cane, or of wood; or of strips of palm tree; and the thickness of each layer should be according to this form and this figure:
However, the layer that is paused over that fracture should be thicker and tighter a little than the other layers; and the length of the layer according to how it lies on the member in greatness and smallness; and then you should tighten over the layers with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then you should tie it decently above it just as we have said about loosening, and let the tightening be less, and how much more the length of the fracture is, the less the tightening.
And let the threads be medium in thickness and thinness, and they should be of linen; and if the threads are thick, just as I have seen through the operation of many foolish doctors tying their threads with cords from a bow, and that is a great error when the tightening is not equal.
And very thin threads are not convenient, because you will not get what you want with them and let there be the thickness of a finger between each bandage.
And if the patient is hurt by the edges of the bandages, then pause below those with cotton or woolen cloth so that it does not harm him.
Sometimes the fracture is in splinters and there occurs disruption in the tendon and I come with a reminder about that in the chapter according to its uniqueness.
And know that not every broken member is tight with bandages on the first day; the cause of which is because the member when it is large, you should not pause over it bandages unless after several days or a week or more, according to your confidence in the occurrence of an ulcer.
And this is the way of the ancient doctors by which they healed those who were broken and dislocated and crooked.
Way of the emplaster which is sprinkled for healing fractures, convenient for many complications and especially for women and children, because it is that from which heat and friction are not felt: and you should take from powder of bran, and it is the membrane of the bran that comes from the movement of the millstone in the mills.

Way to make a poultice that helps injury and pain: take ash, cedar wood shavings or peacock feathers, aloes, and salt, all in equal parts; grind them together and sift them, then mix them, and make a poultice out of it.
But the amount should be according to the extent of the injury and the pain; also, if there is no pain or swelling, nor the bone broken out of its socket, then you should not use this for many days.
