And then adjust the compression little by little, but not as much as at first, and the thickness of the poultice, according to how it has recovered.
But if you see the broken body part already dried and exhausted but not covered, then know that food is prohibited from reaching it; and then soak it with warm water again completely, once every three days; and loosen the poultice a little, and through this operation, the body part receives nourishment, and is healed quickly.

And I have not seen any of them do that, because it is more reasonable that it does not happen.
Chapter according to: Of fractures when they happen in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct, because some fractures are caused by the impact of a staff, and the impact hits all of the bone within the skull, which is beneath the bone, just as it does in the arm, and therefore this type of fracture is called a complete elevation.
And the crack of both these fractures is large or small, and the cause is compression or crushing; and the cause is impact with a stone or hard object, and similar to them.
And this fracture is penetrating that it approaches the membrane which is beneath the bone; or it is in the membrane of the bone, and the crack of both these fractures is large or small.
And from fracture it is hollow in the bone, in the thickness of hair, and because of that this type of fracture is called capillary fracture.
And fracture is caused by impact with a stone or hard object and similar to them, which causes any lamina of the bone to enter between the fingers, and it is made in the hollow place like that happens when it happens to the fingers when there is impact and the lamina of the bone enters between the fingers.
And it happens most frequently in bodies of old men, as well as children's heads.
And all of these species of fractures are set like stars separated. And they will come to the memory of all that in their place.
And you know all these species of fractures by examination and by inquiry from them, and by the appearance of rotten flesh that is on them.
And the species of hair is known by being uncovered over the bone, and washed, and linen is laid on it, because the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fractures, consider first the accidents of the patient, and then if you see through the accidents that indicate by manifest signification fear, as well as vomiting of cholera, extension, loss of understanding, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, emission of humors from those, and appearance of them, and resemblance to them of the accidents in the greater dispositions, it is not healed.
And if you see accidents not running, you expect health, then you begin the cure for him.
The reason for this is that if the afflicted has come to you in the beginning as it is afflicted, and that is in the days of winter, then know that you should study the appearance of the bone before day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then know that you should close up the bone before day seven, having that which is under the skin be corrupted by the bandage, and that the accidents that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the skin covering the brain, and it is in small pieces and rotten, then know that you should insert the rotten small pieces according to what I will recount to you.
And it is that you cut the head of the afflicted, and uncover over the bone according to habit and manner to you possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be lighter over the patient.
And if you see the detection of the bone, blood flows or pus cauterized, then you must oppose it with what is appropriate, and it is that you apply the place with fabrics infused in wine and roasted oil, and make the plague rest while the apostema is settled, and be secure from blood flows; and then begin in the circuit of the bone and in its removal.
And that is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision like this, and this is its shape: And then administer another incision this, a little wider than that; and this is its shape:
And then administer more of the incision wider than the second.
And know that it should be due to you number of different incisions, of which some are wider than others; and some are shorter than others and are in the thickness of their extremities, and are of iron or steel.
And use them with facility in percussion over the incision so that you do not strike the head and lead that.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then know that you pierce in the circuit of it before you administer the incisions with the boring tools, which are named non-deep borers.
And they are not named thus but because those do not stop the end of the bone from that which is under it, for so that the boring tool is rounded on its end or has a margin on that which is under the head of it, acute-like margin or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these boring tools many numbers of which each one is appropriate to the thickness of each bone, while it is to you present in all skulls of the brain whose end is acute, the quantity in length and brevity is according to the quantity of thickness of this skull.
And this is the shape of three species of boring tools: The quality of boring in the circuit of the broken bone is that you stop the boring tool over the bone and turn that with your fingers while you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the boring tool to another location, and pause that which is between all pierces the quantity of thickness of the thread or wire.
And then insert with incisions that which is between each pierce of the bone, and make that more subtle which you can over it in end of elevation, as we have said, while you radiate the bone, or by your hand, or by any other cause through some tools which you have prepared for that, as tweezers and forceps.
And know that you keep with total caution that the incision does not touch any cause of the brain.
And when you have circuit the bone and separated it from the brain if it is continued with it, then extract that, know that cut and gnaw that in its roughness of that which is in the bone with another tool resembling an incision, but however know that it is more thin and more subtle than the other incisions.
And if there appears any part of the small bones and rough, then take that with facility by that which is due to you of the tools; and then cure the plague with poultices and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, farther from fear, which Gualia and Launcelot say by great law; and this is the speech of it: it is necessary to you in the beginning that you begin to uncover the part of the bone of the place in which there is the fracture more violent and more manifest, while when you have uncovered that part and stopped under it the end of this incision long and gentle of which this is the shape:
And the rounded part is lion and do not insert any cause, and the acute part is of it in two sides forward in length, as you see; that the rounded part be fitted to the brain with the membrane of it incisive acute in the bone.
And then pierce over the incision of one part with a small hammer while you insert all the bone with facility as it is turned, and be secure of the membrane that no accident occurs in it which you fear in any manner, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
Then if appears any cause annexed to the bone of the membrane in any place of bone, cut that from it with the end of the incision rounded, and loose that from it with facility, for it is loosed from it without harm and fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate within the membrane, and that which is cut off or is left of the bone is the membrane of it, and appears in it rough and small pieces, then know that you cut that roughness and loose those small pieces with razors subtle by which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it be to you possible to administer of those most convenient ones and the rightest of them according to what you are led astray by your operation that broken bone and its shape.
And know that you administer in your reason with which you cut the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and then use next it of the most subtle of it, and do not cease doing that according to continuity while you come to administer the most thin and the most subtle of all those.
But in each of the other small hair fractures and subtle fracture, know that you cure treatment according to what you are led astray by rectification of it.
And it is caused by not being rubbed on all those who have studied in that art. And we will now show you what we have written about large fractures.
And if the flesh is torn when you remove the bone, then take a piece of cloth according to the amount of the wound and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; then take another piece of folded or doubled cloth and soak it in wine and rose oil and place it on top of the first piece of cloth, and do this gently over the wound so that you don't cause the wound to bleed; then apply a bandage of linen, and don't tighten it but only as much as the wound can take.
And let him stay under this treatment for one or two days until you are sure that the wound won't fester; then loosen the bandage and apply medications to remove excess moisture such as linen rags, oatmeal flour, powdered peas, and similar things.
And it is good for you to make a powder of these medications and sprinkle it on the wound, especially if they are dry; generally speaking, apply to that any medicine of such nature that it cleanses without biting.
And be careful in your treatment that the wound is clean; and let there be no putrescence or anything else on it; nor let virulence accumulate on it; for when it is applied to the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and damages it, and malicious evil happens to the patient.
And it happens in the head when the bone is cleaned and especially when the treatment of it is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; therefore, consider if these accidents happen to the patient as we have said, know that they are all deadly.
And if blackness doesn't happen except from the medicine that is applied to it, and it is because of that medicine that causes the blackness, then take honey in one part and rose oil in three parts, and soak a cloth with them, then place it over the head; and treat that with spices of healing until it is cured.
Chapter 3:
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two pieces except in two cases: either both are upper bones or one of them is cartilage and not broken; and only pain, crookedness, and sinusitis happen to it.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into his nose and grip that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, so that the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and be careful that you don't cause pain to the patient through this operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper bone of the nose and the finger can't reach it, then let the end of the nose be made small and thick.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same thing.
And let him be treated for restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and otherwise, then let him have rest from the festering wound.
And insert into the end of the nose a plug of linen cloth if the fracture is in one part, or two plugs if it's in two parts, and let there be thickness on the plug(s) of an amount that fills the crack in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorators say that the plugs should be wrapped in wool, and changed every day; but I haven't seen this; let there be instead a plug wrapped in honey comfit with oatmeal powder, and let the bone be set and the cartilage harden.
And sometimes there should be inserted into the nose, instead of plugs, a feather of a bird that is wrapped in linen cloth; and let there be such vehemence that the fracture of the nose isn' t prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want to do it, then do it, and if you want to do plugs, then do that.
And if the wound happens again, plaster the nose with cerot or with cotton soaked in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if the wound doesn't happen again, then let there be plastered on the outside with oatmeal flour and powdered peas cooked with honey; and let there be placed over it a plug of linen, and don't wipe the nose for any reason.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or ground down, then split them and extract them with instruments that suit this; then sew up the cut and treat it with something that encourages flesh to grow and harden, and apply plasters as is convenient.
And if the wound happens within the nose, then let it be treated with plugs and lead tubes inserted so that it is completely healed.
Chapter 4:
Of the restoration of the mandible when it is broken.
When the mandible is broken and there is no wound on it, then be careful if the fracture is only on the outside and not broken in two and curved on the inside, then its perception is easier.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger into his mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the mandible on the left, then insert your right index finger; and push with that the crookedness of the fracture inside with ease from the outside with your other hand; with that decently straighten it.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two parts, then let there be extension of those two parts according to straightness so that its position is possible.
And if the teeth are shaken by disruption, then strengthen those of which you expect that they will break with thread of gold or silver or of silk; and let there be applied to the broken mandible cerot, and let a doubled cloth be placed over it, and place over the cloth a great star decently made, or a sole piece as long as the mandible; and tie that under it according to how you are equipped with its application, so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to have rest and stillness, and let him drink wine mixed with rose oil.
And if you see that its shape is altered in any way, then let it be checked and loosened on the third day; and straighten what has been altered on it, and plaster it with oatmeal flour, with honey, or with oatmeal flour, after you have removed the cerot from it, and place over the plaster wool of linen.
And then after that dressing is placed on it and the position of the bone isn't altered, let it be left so that it's healed and the fracture strengthened; for many times this fracture has been healed in three weeks.
And if the wound happens again, then let there be done what we have said several times in its healing so that it' s healed, and let that wound be cleaned.
And if the fracture has a wound, then consider if it is split or shattered, then you be subtle so that those pieces with what suits for this in the removal of those tools.
And if the opening of the wound is small, then you should make it larger with a bloodletting needle according to the amount of your need.
Then, when you have stopped the bleeding and it does not bleed again, then open the wound if it is large; but if not, put one of the ointments that are fit for this purpose onto it and cover it until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Many times the collarbone is broken in front of the chest, and that fracture happens in one of three ways.
When it is broken, either it is separated or it is separated into two parts without any bleeding and it is easier to restore, or it happens with bleeding and it is more difficult to restore, or the fracture occurs through the skin.
And the operation on that, when the fracture is through the skin, is that you have two ministers present, so that one has the aid and follows the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck in that direction.
Then fasten the fracture with your fingers until it is made in its form according to how it should be, and there is not a little protuberance or concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you should put a pillow of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit, and its size according to the amount of your need; and extend and raise the collarbone and narrow the pillow with your hand until it fastens the fracture as it should be.
And if you cannot bring the end of the collarbone to the sides outside because it is reached by the armpit, then you should have the patient lean back on his neck and place a cushion under the muscle on that side.
And the minister should press the muscle on that side towards the sides down so that the bone of the collarbone is raised up where it is in the armpit, and then you should straighten the fracture and fasten it with your fingers.
But if you feel bleeding from the collarbone and they are not stopped, then you should open it and stop those bleedings with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision on it with one of the cutting instruments which you have prepared for this purpose, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument which serves the head; and that instrument is of wood or iron, and this is the shape of it:
And it is similar to a spoon of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you need it for, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends thus, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound which you have incised when you have extracted the bleeding from the bone is wide, and it is certain that there will be a scab, then you should bring the two lips of the wound together with a suture.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a scab, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the cut of the wound.
And if a scab occurs, then soak a cloth in warm vinegar and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And let it not bleed on the broken bone; and so that you see the patient every day, when the bandage becomes soft or you see the stake has bled, then you should straighten that and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient's pillow under his neck, and place a small cushion under the armpit on that side, so that with that the support of it is raised from that side, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone is elevated by the elevation of the muscle; or let his arm hang at his neck.
And do not loosen the bandage unless bleeding occurs in the site or a scab, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster when you have seen his face to that, and adjust the fastening and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comfortable according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The scapula is broken in its lateral part and not broken from it but the margins.
And then when it is broken in its middle part and not broken from it but the margins, then according to how the fracture is twisted you should prepare it and reduce it according to the natural shape in every way that is possible for you.
And then place on the site powder of wheat flour with egg white and a poultice, and then place on it a bandage of cloth doubled; and then place on it a slender stake of wood according to the quantity of the scapula or more.
But if under the stake the scapula is hollowed out in any of its parts, then you should straighten that hollow with a poultice, and then place the stake according to equality.
And then tighten above it with a long bandage with mild compression and the stake so that it is not bled from its site.
And consider the bandage every day; and whenever it becomes soft, loosen another turn and tighten it and equalize the stake if it has bled from its site.
And support the patient on his side.
And the scapula is restored in 20 days or in 25.
And then when that number is complete, then you should dissolve the bandage, and know that it is certain that there is a beginning of union of those bones from which there is not feared or their dissolution.
And if the site of the bone weighs down and there is placed under it the cord, then you should cut that, and support it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the collarbone about bleeding from a scab if it occurs here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in its middle part and that especially occurs at the end; but the ends of it are usually crushed and ground together.
And when the patient's lung is broken it leads to a decline in the internal parts and the occurrence of great pain, difficulty in breathing, cough, and strong vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is revealed and not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration of the patient is that he should be supine on his back, and place a cushion between his shoulders; and press the muscles of his chest, and adjust his ribs with the hands of two assistants, and act subtly in the operation and according to all ways that are possible and convenient, until the bone returns to its shape.
And then place a plaster and wadding over the fracture site, and put on top of it a thin table made of willow or alder wood, or similar materials in levitation, wrapped in cloth; and act subtly in tying that around the broken bone so that there is no pain; and go with the binding according to the shape of the back several times, and tighten that decently with a strap; and consider the binding at all times, and when it becomes soft, tighten it.
And if necessity requires the removal of that bandage or dressing, or swelling, or pain, or abscess that occurs in the place, then you cut and loosen that, and remove the plaster and correct what occurs through the mode of cure of the patient; and then return the plaster, if possible, to its former state; and tighten the binding so that it is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that rib fractures do not occur except in large places that follow the back; and from their ends at the front, contriction does not occur to those except through what are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not hidden from sensation by means of inquiry with the fingers.
And the restoration is that you unite the fracture with your fingers according to the possible mode, so that it is united according to its shape; and then plaster and tighten the broken bone with splints, if needed.
And if the rib fracture is declining to the internal parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and puncturing, just as puncturing occurs in one who has pleurisy, and it causes difficulty in breathing, cough, and vomiting of blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the Antics are ingeniously devised for that with many ingenious things.
And from them I say: place foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and extended, and the fracture is pushed to the external parts.
But we should eat that so that it is not a cause to generate an abscess, if it has not occurred; and if it has occurred, then it helps.
And some of them say that it should be placed above the windpipe and that it should draw strength; and it is like reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the windpipe will send the superfluities to the place due to weakness.
And some of them say: cover the site with wool that is already submerged in hot oil, and place powders between the ribs so that it is filled, and let the binding be made as round as you wrap it; and then the patient should be cured with pleurisy cure by food and medicine.
And if it causes vehement constriction on the patient who cannot bear it, and the bone punctures the membrane with piercing pain, then fear for the patient; cover the site, cut open the broken rib, and place over it an instrument that conserves the heart, the remembrance of which is preceded, so that we may insert it into the bone with ease and extract it.
And then tie the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and cure it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs within, then you cut and pour powders into rose oil and place it over the site, and let the patient be cured by that which occurs to the abscess within.
And he should be laid down on his side on which he sleeps so that it is healed.
Chapter 9.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and collarbone.
Sometimes it is a fracture of the bones of the collarbone, and it hurts them there and the joints of the spine. And if it happens to someone and you want to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider that if you see the joints soft and stupified and dead, and they cannot move them nor tighten them, and when you press them with your fingers or puncture them with an awl and do not feel pain in those places, then know that it is not healed; and in some dispositions it is deadly.
And if they hurt and you feel constriction in those places with the fingers or puncture, then know that the joint of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with cure.
And if you see the joints of the collarbone like this and want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that they are already soft and it happens to them what happened to the joints, and then when he lies on his back, he experiences windiness and involuntary urination, and sometimes he is laid on his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is deadly, and so you should not treat him.
But if none of those happen, the cause is better and less distant.
And the cure of him is that you pierce into the abscess that you place over the fractured joint with a dropper, using either rose oil solution or crushed beans; place it on several times throughout the day so that the abscess is soothed.
And then place one of the comforting and relaxing plasters, and tighten it with a strap; and order the patient to take rest and tranquility and not sleep on the side that hurts so that he is healed.
And if it occurs due to the joint of the bone being separated from him, then cut over the cord and let the bone rest; and then unite the two lips of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then cure it with something encrusted from within the gums, so that it is healed.
But if it is the end of the bone of the hip joints and the end of the cloth, then insert your left index finger into the anus, and unite the broken bone with the other hand according to what is possible by the second mode; and then place a plaster and splint over it, if needed, and then tighten that.
And if you feel broken tendons in him, then cut over it and hold it, and cure the wound with the cure that is preceded so that it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the shoulder joint.
The bones of the shoulder joints hurt when they are broken, and if they are broken, then their fracture is only a conjunction in their extremities, and they are inserted lengthwise and decline to the internal parts; and it hurts the patient in that place with piercing pain.
And the restoration of him is that you extend your hand over him so that it is over the fracture as is his shape.
And if the fracture is only in its extremity, then you unite it according to what appears to you by the second mode so that it is assembled into its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the leg or that which inclines towards the parts below, then you make the patient lie on his belly until he is prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have bound that up, place a plaster on it, and also place a splint of wood or cane on it, and tighten it with a bandage so that there will be no puncturing of the fracture or removal of the splinter.
And fill the cavity with something that fills it up so that it assumes its normal shape, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if an abscess has occurred, then cease from its extension and restoration until the abscess is suppurated, and place on it something that promotes suppuration according to what has been mentioned before; then return to its restoration, and bind it tightly with a bandage in that way.
And if the bones have become separated or splintered, then you treat them.
Chapter 11.
On the restoration of the fracture of the hip.
The hip is what is between the armpit and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a splint of the thickness of a finger according to this form:
And let there be holes at both ends of the two splints, and let it be suspended from an elevated point.
And let the patient lie on a chair; and let him extend his broken arm over the splint until he reaches the tip of it at the point where the curve of the splint is attached.
And let someone suspend some heavy weight over it, or the assistant extend the arm downward; and then the doctor applies pressure with his hands on both sides of the fracture until the fracture goes back into place according to how it should be.
And the other way is that the patient be laid on his neck and suspend his hand from his collar with a splint; and let the doctor command two servants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other holds below the patient's arm; and each of them extends to his own side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten with a splint on the fracture and another splint above it, and let each of the two servants extend to his side.
And if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then it should be placed halfway on the tip and the other half below the fracture beyond the armpit.
And similarly if the fracture is near the armpit, then let the splint be placed on that point and on the armpit.
Bind that up with ease and without pain, and then when it is bound according to how it should be and applied well, tighten it if an abscess does not occur.
But if an abscess occurs, then cease from tightening it until the seventh day, and place on it soft wool soaked in wine and rose oil until the abscess drains; then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster on the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then lift up the arm on that hip, and place your open hand on his muscle.
And let the cloth and arm rest on the hip so that the arm is in its natural place, unless some cause from the broken bone prohibits it.
Then use splints.
And it is that you place on the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the others; and let there be between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers of all parts.
And then tighten on the splints with the binding which is described at the beginning of the chapter.
And let your binding on the site of the fracture be tighter. And when the fracture is longer, then let the binding be shorter.
And so if you see the position of the splints and binding as we have said in the time when you are restoring the member to its natural position, then you do this:
And if you fear an abscess, then you cease from tightening and splints for the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the splint in all parts for three days so that nothing occurs at the point of pressure or inflammation, or that nourishment does not reach the member because of a surplus of binding; and then rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are certain that nothing occurs of that, then do not loosen the ligature except after three or more days.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his hands be over his stomach.
And place on the hip a poultice full of wool bound up, and consider all night or day that it is not loosened or dissolved nor the figure of the broken member altered, or that the binding be softened; then rectify all that with your skill.
And let the patient' s food be according to the order which we have mentioned before, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it is started being tightened; then let the food become coarser.
The custom of the hip and the bed is that they are tightened for 40 days; then let them be loosened and a bath be administered, and it be anointed with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid open, then you do not loosen from it the binding and splints until 10 or 2 days.
Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is made of two bones which are called two bones; one of which is small and is that which supports the finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small finger from the part that is further away.
And usually it is only the larger bone or the smaller one that is broken.
And when the small bone is broken above, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is easier.
And when it is broken below, then the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone which is broken is the small bone above, then it is convenient for the doctor, in order to restore it, to place a little extension with ease so that it is bound up.
But if the larger bone is broken, then let there be placed a more violent extension.
And if both bones are broken at the same time, then let you place the most violent extension.
And let the figure of the hand be according to its restoration and its extension stretched out over a pillow, and let its wrist be above the parts, more above all the other fingers, and let the finger be below all the others, and let the patient lie on his back on a inclined pillow.
And let one pillow be in front of him at an angle or straight under him so that the patient does not strain himself.
And then the assistant extends the arm of the part below with his hand or with a splint, and another assistant extends it above similarly.
And then the doctor binds the bone so that he reduces it according to the best figure that he can.
And if there are sores on the bones, then you must pierce through and reduce all pain in its place with your virtue.
And if there appears on it a separate sore that punctures the cord and is not hopeful for its restoration, then you must cut over it and treat it according to the manner we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a particular chapter about it, and so take its cure from that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then you must line a cloth with ceratum made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thin and thick, and tie the tight cloth over that so that the abscess may yield; and then remove the ceratum and place an ointment prepared from moly powder with honey, and then place stelae; and let the stela which is placed over that fracture be more pus-laden and stronger.
And know that the number of the stelae on the arm is seen in the greater dispositions, or it is a fracture in one or two joints together.

Chapter 20
On the treatment of nodules that appear on the site of a fracture.
This nodule often occurs on the site of a healed fracture, especially near the joints, and affects the shape of the limb; it strongly hinders the limb from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, if the nodule is recent, administer to it those medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloe, myrrh, and gall-nut; take some or all of these, and prepare them with white wine, or vinegar, or sweet wine, and apply the mixture to the nodule using a piece of cloth, and bind it tightly in place.
And let the binding remain in place for several days without removing it; then loosen it and repeat the treatment, until the nodule is reduced in size; or press a piece of lead gently over it, since lead has the property of reducing anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the nodule has already turned into a stone and it is causing pain, then split it at its highest point, and drain out the pus and any other fluid that has accumulated inside it; treat this wound so that it heals.
Chapter 21
On the restoration of a fracture when it is healed, and on the subsequent return of the limb to its normal condition, without any deformity.
When a bone fracture is healed, and the limb regains its normal condition without any deformity, this happens due to several causes: one cause is excessive fluid retention in the ligament and the failure to properly bind it; another cause is excess swelling of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive movement at an inappropriate time; or a deficiency of blood in the patient's body, or weakness.
The treatment for this is to encourage the patient to eat and make their heart strong, so that blood flow is increased in them; administer a bath; and apply poultices of marjoram and lettuce over the affected area.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that it draws nutrients to the area; or continue to apply warm water to it, until the flow of nutrients is restored and the limb returns to its normal shape.
Chapter 22
On the treatment of fractured bones that are healed in a crooked position, and prevent them from returning to their normal condition.
When a healed bone is crooked and there is nodule formation, causing distortion in the shape of the limb, but the limb can still function normally, then you must not accept the word of someone who claims that this limb has recently been fractured.

And sometimes it is dissolved by continuous gentle friction with the hands that are moistened, and the limb should be moved to all sides at all times.
But if the crookedness has progressed further and has become a stone, requiring treatment with iron, then the pus should be drained first, and the bone should be softened by dissolving any nodule with subtle incisions, and the area should be treated with gentle manipulation and attention.
Chapter 23
On speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the separation of a joint, causing the limb to be unable to move, and the shape of the limb is altered, bringing great pain and severe injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it must be reduced immediately and not delayed, for if it is delayed, the joint will become inflamed, and its reduction will then be very difficult.
And for that reason, it must not be bent or twisted during the process of reduction, for this often causes spasms and pain.
But when this happens, the patient should be given a bath, and then the affected area should be left to rest until the swelling subsides; and the limb should be bathed in warm water with oil, and then reduced gently.
And every limb should be treated according to its location.
And I have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, following those on fracture, from the highest part of the body to the lowest.
Chapter 24

Chapter .XXVII.
On the curing of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the joint of the elbow is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and especially to the front part from the back part, and its dislocation is not concealed from you because when you try to bend the dislocated elbow towards you or away from you depending on how it is dislocated, this will be made manifest to you, for the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm or touch with that muscle.

And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce, is that the hand of it be stretched with two hands, and the arm of it expanded, and the doctor's hands on the elbow and under it, and he pushes the joint together with his fingers or the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front part, then it returns thus that he bends the hand with his hand, while striking the elbow with the root of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer vigorous extension and great force.
And two ministers are to hold that joint while it is being extended so that it is not pulled back by the extension.
And then let the arm be rotated on all sides, for it is wrapped in a long folded cloth over the hand or ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should smear it with some oil so that it causes the cause of the joint to be helped by ease of lubrication with facility; and then push the joint together with strong impulsion until it returns.
And then its reduction should be that the poultice in which it is wrapped with stiptic and astringent albumen of egg white, and it being tightened by descent binding, and the arm suspended from the neck, and let go for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen its binding and leave it.
And if you see the joint not to be stronger, then you iterate the poultice and binding, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then loosen it.
But if it happens to it heaviness after the reduction of the joint, and its disposition is in movement, then administer moisture in bath and subtle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or pause over the joint all-night-oil, as it is grease of motion; and then let it be bound, and leave that on the bone the day and night. And then wash that off, and let him take a bath; and when he is bathed, you rub the joint with tempered friction, and then iterate over it all-night-oil, as it is rose-oil of motion, once, and the second, and the iii.a., Corruption has already occurred on the nerve through the instrument of bathing, unless it was limited.
And if you want, pause over that cow's udder moistened with wine and heat over it; and do that several times, for that is moist and reduced to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
On the curing of dislocation of a mallet, that is of the joint which is between the arm and hand.
The hand some times is dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easier, but it is contrary with other joints; however, when there is swiftness in its reduction at the hour that it is dislocated, before the place becomes ulcerated or that an ulcerated wound occurs on it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the mallet on a table and the minister extends the hand of him, and the doctor pauses his hand or his palm over the impendence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
But however, let it be considered if the palm is already turned to the belly of the hand, then the mallet should place both hands on a table because of extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is before the palm of the hand, then the position of the hand of him intrinsecly over the table, so that the hand of the doctor rests on that impendence of the joint.
And then if it returns at once, good; and if not, then loosen that with poultice, letting it go; and do not repeat that, for unless it passes on it days, you will not suffer nor be able to reduce it.
But however, let the joint be reduced according to its tortuosity; and do not trouble the mallet for any cause, unless the hand is softened and not able to strengthen for any cause; then know that the nerves are severed or torn, and then it does not have strength in it except when it is tightened with cautery close to the joint of it and very often that confers no cause.
Then when the mallet is returned, then pause on it the poultice which we have recommended, and let it be left and tightened for several days; and then loosen it and let the hand of him be exercised.
But if its movement is stopped and any heaviness happens to it, then loosen that with water and with friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter .XXIX.
On the curing of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes they are completely displaced.
And when any of these fingers are dislocated on the back of the hand or on the belly, then press the finger and push out its dislocation with your thumbs, while returning it to its position.
Then hold the head of the finger and suspend that part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it while straightening it on that day.
And when morning comes, apply that narrative again, and do not cease loosening it during the day and exercising with movement, and apply it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight.
And do this similarly if it is dislocated on the belly of the hand, and apply that narrative to that part and do your first operation with it until it is healed.
And similarly do for when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and many vertebrae have left their places, then there is no cure for that, for death overtakes the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excrement escapes without their will, and they cannot retain it; and many times some members of them or both legs of them or both arms of them, or one of those, are deadened, because many times a vertebra has left its place and many times there is removal of those to four parts.
So that is already deadened in the back parts and is named paralysis.
But its cure is that then you consider if the paralysis occurred from youth, because in that there is no cure nor any healing of that at all.
But in that which comes from falling or from beating or similarly to those, the Ancients have already described the methods of cure with a lengthy speech, of which the most returns with profit; but I and shorten that from which little excuse of it from its excess for me, because of my proximity to my intention my exposition, by which I have already exposed and formed from diverse instrument of that by which I will show and spread that.
And of those there is no cure except that which occurs properly in the back by that which I relate.
And it is that the patient be extended on his face over a pillow propped up near a wall, and be extended under him instrument wet so that his feet are not bound.
And then a straight stick be placed, set in the ground at the end of the pillow around the head of him, and another stick in the air under his feet at the other end of the pillow.
And the minister hold the stick, and it not be stopped in a pit; and another minister hold the other second that form.
And then wrap around the head of the patient and under them tickle with a lion tail; and extend the end of the tail to the stick by which it is supposed the head of him, and apply that on him; and then tighten all the tails and apply those on the other stick by which it is supposed the feet of him.
And then the ministers each extend the tail to the end of the stick, and not be deadened the two sticks and the tails in their locations in which they are placed, but nevertheless do not stop them as we have said.
And the doctor places his palms on the vertebra with strength until it returns, or that he places on a table, and then he adjusts over the table his feet until he returns.
But if he does not return by this form, then take a table of which the breadth or the length is in the air at three arms' length, and dig into the wall of which we have said that it is near the patient a place in which between the end of the table. And then place the middle of the table on the vertebra, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and tighten so that the vertebra is cramped and returns to its location.
And if you want, open by a turn which is wound with the hand; and it is that you place on the ground beneath the head of the patient at the end of the pillow two sticks, the length of which is one arm, and place the head of him in the end of the pillow two sticks; and let the length be between each stick one palm, and it is already made in each stick a hole in which turn, and each be very stopped on the ground so that it not be deadened.
And let there be a round stick in which is the turn, and it is wound in the two holes of the two sticks.
And in its end is a hole in which the stick is formed, the length of which is one palm by which it is wound; and in the two other sticks is similarly.
And then tighten the tails which you have tightened on the feet of the patient in the hole by which it is supposed the head of him, and the tails which you have tightened on his thighs in the turn by which it is supposed the feet of him.
And then each turn and stand a minister who twists the turn with his hand, and the doctor smooths the vertebra as we have said.
And then when the vertebra has returned and the location is smoothed, then cover that with plaster of egg white, and then stop; and then place over the plaster a cloth of table of which the breadth is three fingers or more, and the length of it is quantity that takes up the location of the paralysis, and over the end of the vertebra its.
And then let it be tied with a tail as we have said, and use the patient of subtle food until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the location at the end of the healing, then cover that with medicines that soften and spread by administration of the table for a long time, which we have recommended; and it is administered in that location leaf of lead.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the end of the vertebrae of the back, and it is seized by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells so that it not be made projection to him by this medicine, because strongly he will do to you occur death.
Chapter 31.
Of cure of hip dislocated.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not deviate but dislocation only, and it does not occur to those that occur to the other joints of small removal and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four ways, because it is dislocated at the ends inside, or at the ends outside, or at the ends in front, or at the ends behind.
And most often it is dislocated at the ends inside, and seldom at the ends in front or at the ends behind.
And the sign of its dislocation at the ends inside is that when you press the healthy hip with the sick, it is longer and the tendon is more prominent than the healthy, and the patient cannot bend his leg because of the lengthening, and it is the location which they say the lengthening of the tendon causes apothema cauter, so that the head of the hip has already come here.
And the sign of him to whom this dislocation happens is that his parts are bent outward, and that his accidents are contrary to this accident.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation happens in front is that he stretches his leg according to his ability, but still feels pain where there is pain; and if he attempts to walk and cannot go forward in front, and his urine is retained and his testicles are swollen, and he has difficulty walking on the ball of his foot.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation happens in back is that he cannot straighten his leg or bend the testicles forward, and his leg is shorter than the other, and the testicles are softened, and the head of the thigh points to that location.
But the reduction of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, that it has been long since it was contracted, and that you should not administer reduction to it, but only when it has been recently contracted; then there is no need for poultices.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four species of dislocation, then you go to that which turns the joint and extends it within and without, and from both right and left, for he will quickly return and does not need other treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare to extend the leg of him from the lowest part, or with his own hands, or by means of a bandage which is tied over the leg above the thigh; and another extends his hand of that one above the ankle so that he may put his fingers under the toes; and another tightens a lion skin around the root of the thigh, and let another minister hold the end of the lion skin.
And it should be extended either from within to the anus, or from behind to the navel.
And all of these should be extended in one sitting while the patient is elevated from the ground and his leg hangs down.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary to all species that we speak of the proper treatment.
In the proper reduction of it, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is within, let the patient be laid on his side; and then place the lion skin over the root of the thigh in that place between the head of the thigh and the location below the testicles; and then extend the lion skin above the ankle to the highest part of the breastbone.
And then another minister should take hold of his arms and press the thick part of the bad thigh and extend to the outside by violent extension, for it will return to its place.
And this species is the quickest cure by which that member is reduced compared to the other species.
But if he is stubborn towards you and does not obey this species of treatment at all, then let his two feet be bound together with a strong bandage, quickly, over both balls and both thighs.
And let the length of each of his parts be the quantity of four digits, and let the bad thigh be extended more than the other by a quantity of two digits.
And then let the bad leg be suspended over the head of the beam and be in the middle; and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to lessen the head of the thigh, and let another young man suspend it, and the other young man lessen the thigh with strength push, for the joint will return to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when it is dislocated outside, let the patient lie on his back according to what we have recommended regarding the swelling; and let the lion skin be tightly applied over the bad thigh properly and over the feet of him.
And then let two beams be placed, one under his feet and the other under his head; and then let beams be placed close to the middle of the pile upon which dampened cloths are wrapped, so that the bad leg is not harmed by the beam being between his thighs, so that the extension below is not pulled.
And then each minister should extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand should direct the reduction according to the article.
And when the dislocation is in front, then let the leg be extended quickly, and it is according to this narrative on the pile; and the doctor let the palm of his right hand rest over the testicles, and then tighten that with his other hand, and with that tightening let him extend that to the lowest part towards the navel.
And if the dislocation is behind, then let the bad leg not be extended to the lowest part and let it be elevated above the ground; but let him lie on a hard cause, likewise as if his thigh is dislocated outside towards two beams according to what we have said about the sitting of him on two beams; and let him lie on his back and the lion skin be extended, likewise as we have said, no doubt.
And let compression be administered with a table over the location at which the joint goes.
Therefore when the reduction is complete according to what you have — and the sign of the reduction of him is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend two thighs, and when you see those bumps and the bad leg stretch its thigh and expand likewise without excuse, then know that the member according to which it has returned — and then you adjust two thighs and apply poultice and tighten with bandage thus so that the thigh is not bent in any of the parts; and let the bad leg rest for three days or four.
And then loosen the lion skin and the poultice, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see those bumps in truth, then know that the dislocation is already stopped, and then let the bad leg walk.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and poultice that, and tighten that according to your first extension, and let him rest for three days.
And then loosen that, and let him delay walking on it for several days, within which he is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the testicles.
The testicles are dislocated according to three ways, for it is dislocated outside and inside and below but not behind; and in front it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of him is that you command the bad leg to apply his thigh to his thigh; then know that the testicles are dislocated.
And the restoration of all his ways is that you make the bad leg straight, thus so that it extends its thigh, if it can above that; adhere your two arms to his face, and let him place his foot between his feet; and then let him adhere the testicles with your palms, and press those between your fingers over the testicles of him with strength, and another minister extend his foot thus so that the testicles return to their place.
And a sign that the patient is improving is when their bed does not adhere to the mattress with effort.
Then plaster that area and attach the bed to the mattress, then bind those areas with a bandage for three or four days, then loosen them.
And do not let the patient walk for several days until they are healed and comfortable.
But if you cannot reduce the dislocation, then administer strong extension with instruments of which mention was made in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of a dislocation of the talus.
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a small displacement and sometimes dislocated according to its completion; its dislocation can be made either within or without the parts.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you will see the talus swollen in the direction of the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a small displacement of this is gentle reduction; it should be extended easily with the hands and bound until it returns.
But in the treatment of this when it is dislocated according to its completion, you should make the patient lie on his back; then have a strong servant pull firmly upward from behind at the patient's ankle in the middle of that location.
Then you should have your right hand hold the foot of the highest part and your left hand hold the lowest part of the foot at the location of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and twice with the left, then extend with the left; push the bottom of the foot with your right hand towards the bed in front of the thigh.
And do this twice according to what we have told you.
Then push the bottom of the foot against the bed on the third attempt; then you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two attempts according to this method and you see the foot swelling, that is good; but if not, then repeat the operation, for it will return.
But if you cannot reduce it by what we have commanded, then let the patient lie on his back on the ground, and tie a rope firmly to the ground, and place it between the patient's thighs; then you have wrapped a cloth around him so that he does not slide.
Then let one servant hold the patient's thighs and another extend the foot with their hands or with an instrument they have placed over the neck of the foot; then each servant should extend against the other, and the rope be pulled between the patient's thighs so that his body is not drawn to the lower parts due to the extension.
Then let the doctor hold the dislocation with his hands; then let another servant hold the healthy thigh at the lower parts, for the dislocation will return with ease.
And so when the dislocation returns and you see that it is healed by the reduction of it, then apply plaster and extend it with strong instruments, and bind the foot to the bed.
And beware of the nerves which are behind the talus, so that the instrument over it is not violent and does not strike it forcefully.
Then leave it for two or three days; if the instrument softens, loosen it.
Then loosen it on the third day or fourth; then forbid the patient to walk for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk within that space, it is not safe that the dislocation is not dissolved and ruined, and then what treatment is not received.
And if an ulcer occurs to him, then you should administer in the healing of it what we have commanded before, in another place for treatment and brothy, so that it is heated.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Let that which is dislocated be swollen with little extension without effort; and that which is difficult, first be gentle.
But if the dislocation is in some part of the top of the foot, then let the patient lie on their back over something flat on the ground or on a table, and be lying as if they were walking.
Then you stand on your feet and place your foot over that which protrudes from the joints; then press that with your foot with force so that it returns; then see that it is already swollen and does not appear as a protuberance in that location.
Then place under the bottom of his foot a board which covers all of the foot, to which are two ends, and then tighten that with decent and firm pressure for three days; then loosen it and let him rest from walking for several days so that it is relaxed and its reduction is assured.
Chapter 35.
On the species of dislocation which are made with splint or with fracture or with any other impression.
When any of those things happen and you are expert in the treatment of that and in the restoration of it, many times death succeeds.
And therefore you should not presume over the treatment of it, but that which is wise in the art, from long experience, merciful and compassionate, slow, not hasty or presumptuous; and use in the beginning of medicines which produce ulcers cauterized only, and let the patient go to divine judgement, unless you hope for salvation from destruction, with some relief of illness, and in which there appears some hope. And then effort to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it produces an ulcerated cauterized.
And then if the member returns according as we want, then administer a regimen which yields an ulcerated cauterized and cures the splint with what is convenient for it from the dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with fracture and occurs in the bones separated, then you are expert at their binding, and do in that which we have said in the simple illnesses of those.
And beware and avoid according to your strength that of the instrument in ways of fear according as you are preceded by my prediction; for that is better for you to your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthy to see those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly of which God be blessed. Amen.
